Fifth Objection: Imam as Preserver of Sharia Cannot Err
The author refutes the argument that the Imam's role as preserver of Sharia precludes error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Imam cannot err because he is the preserver of Sharia, and the response that his preservation is mediated through scripture and community.

Core Idea
The Imam preserves Sharia through means, not by his essence.

This lesson tackles a common sectarian objection: if the Imam is the preserver of Sharia, how can he err? The author refutes this by clarifying the nature of the Imam's role — he is not an infallible guardian but a scholar who relies on the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and correct ijtihad. The lesson also defines the dialectical term 'naqḍ' and its types.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of argument.
2
Identify the key argument
Notice the objector's claim that error would violate preservation. Then see the author's answer: preservation is not by essence.
3
Understand the technical term 'naqḍ'
The footnote defines naqḍ. Compare its linguistic and technical meanings.
4
Reflect on the role of community
The response mentions mujtahids and those commanding good as safeguards. Consider how this diffuses the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - أنه حافظ للشريعة . فلوجاز الخطأ عليه لكان ناقضاً لها حافظاً فيعود على موضوعه بالنقض .
والجواب أنه ليس حافظاً لها بذاته بل بالكتاب والسنة، وإجماع الأمة، واجتهاده الصحيح . فإن أخطأ في اجتهاده أو ارتكب معصية فالمجتهدون يردون، والأمرون بالمعروف يصدون وإن لم يفعلوا أيضاً، فلا نقض(٢) للشريعة القويمة، ولا نقض على الطريقة المستقيمة .
(٢) النقض لغة : الكسر ، وفي الاصطلاح هو بيان تخلف الحكم المدعي بثبوته أو نفيه عن دليل المسلل نقضاً الدال عليه في بعض من الصور فإن وقع بمنع شيء من مقدمات الدليل على الإجمال سمي إجمالياً لأن حاصله يرجع إلى منع شيء من مقدمات الدليل على الإجمال، وإن وقع بالمنع المجرد، أو مع السند سمي نقضاً تفصيلياً لأنه منع مقدمة معينة . وقيل النقض: وجود العلة بلا حكم .
The fifth: That he is the preserver of the Sharī‘ah. So if error were permissible upon him, he would be violating it while being its preserver, so the subject would be undermined by the violation (naqḍ).
The answer: That he is not a preserver of it by his essence, but rather by the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the community, and his correct ijtihād. So if he errs in his ijtihād or commits a sin, then the mujtahids reject it, and those who command right prevent it, and even if they do not do so, there is no violation of the upright Sharī‘ah, and no violation of the straight path.
(2) al-Naqḍ: In language, breaking. In technical terminology, it is the demonstration of the non-occurrence of the claimed ruling (thabūt or nafī) from the evidence of the reasoning (al-musallal?) in some cases. If it occurs by the prevention of one of the premises of the evidence in general, it is called 'total' (ijmālī) because its result reverts to prevention of a premise. If it occurs by pure prevention or with a chain, it is called 'detailed' (tafsīlī) naqḍ. And it is said: al-Naqḍ is the existence of the cause without the ruling.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its refutation. The objector argues that the Imam is the preserver of the Sharī‘ah, so if he errs, he would contradict his own role, thus undermining the subject. The response states that the Imam does not preserve the Sharī‘ah by his own essence but through the Book, Sunnah, consensus of the community, and his correct ijtihād. If he errs or sins, the mujtahids reject his action and those commanding good prevent it. Even if they do not act, there is no violation (naqḍ) of the Sharī‘ah. The footnote defines naqḍ as a technical term in dialectics: it is showing that a ruling does not follow from the evidence in some cases. It distinguishes between total (ijmālī) and detailed (tafsīlī) naqḍ.

Key Terms
حافظ
نقض
اجتهاد
إجماع
كتاب
سنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam is personally infallible and preserves Sharia by his own authority.
Correction: The response clarifies that the Imam preserves through delegated means (Book, Sunnah, consensus, ijtihad) and is not personally infallible.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the fifth objection, how does the Imam preserve the Sharī‘ah?
A) By his own infallibility and divine protection
B) By the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and his correct ijtihād
C) By performing miracles and having knowledge of the unseen
D) By being chosen by the community without error

Key Takeaway
The Imam's preservation of Sharia is not personal but mediated through scripture, consensus, and correct ijtihad; error in ijtihad does not undermine the Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you place your trust in a religious authority, do you treat their words as if they were the Sharia itself, or do you check them against the Book, Sunnah, and consensus? How might this lesson reshape your attitude toward scholars?
?
If you discovered that a respected imam or teacher made a serious mistake in a ruling, would it shake your confidence in the religion? According to this passage, where does the real preservation of Sharia lie, and how does that free you from putting humans on a pedestal?
?
Think of a time you accepted a religious ruling without verifying its sources. How can you now ensure that your practice is anchored in the means of preservation—the Book, Sunnah, and sound reasoning—rather than in the persona of a leader?

Resolving the Dilemma of Obeying vs. Denouncing the Sinful Imam
The author resolves the apparent contradiction between obeying and denouncing the Imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent contradiction between obeying and denouncing a sinful Imam is resolved in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Obedience to authority is conditional; sin must be denounced.

This lesson addresses a common theological dilemma: if we are commanded to obey those in authority, how can we also denounce their sins? The author presents the objection and provides a clear resolution by distinguishing between lawful and unlawful commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own words come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice that the objector presents two possibilities: either denouncing is obligatory (which contradicts obedience) or it is not obligatory (which contradicts scripture and consensus). Each leads to a problem.
The dilemma is a classic logical device.
3
Understand the answer that resolves the dilemma
The author specifies that the obligation to obey is limited to matters that do not contravene the Sharia. For sinful acts, rejection and denunciation are required, or silence if not feasible.
This answer dissolves the contradiction by qualifying the scope of obedience.
4
Reflect on the conditionality of obedience
Consider the broader principle: obedience to any authority, including the Imam, is never absolute. It is always conditioned by the limits set by divine law.
This is a key principle in Islamic political theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - أنه لو أقدم على المعصية، فإما أن يجب الإنكار عليه وهو مضاد لوجوب إطاعته الثابت بقوله تعالى أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم فيلزم اجتماع الضدين، وإما أن لا يجب وهو خلاف النص والإجماع . والجواب أن وجوب الطاعة إنما هو فيما لا يخالف الشرع . وأما فيما يخالفه فالرد والإنكار ، وإن لم يتيسر فسكوت عن اضطرار.
The sixth: That if he commits a sin, then either it is obligatory to denounce him, which contradicts the obligation to obey him established by His saying 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (4:59), so there would be a union of two opposites; or it is not obligatory, which is contrary to text and consensus.
The answer: The obligation to obey is only in that which does not contravene the Sharī‘ah. As for that which contravenes it, then rejection and denunciation, and if it is not feasible, then silence due to duress.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection and its refutation. The objector poses a dilemma: if the Imam commits a sin, then either it is obligatory to denounce him, which contradicts the obligation to obey him (based on the verse), leading to a contradiction; or it is not obligatory, which is against scripture and consensus. The response resolves the dilemma by specifying that obedience is due only in matters that do not contravene the Sharī‘ah. In matters that contravene it, rejection and denunciation are required; if denunciation is not possible, then silence due to duress is permissible.

Key Terms
الإنكار
وجوب الطاعة
الضدين
معصية
أولي الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obedience to the Imam is absolute and unconditional.
Correction: Obedience is only required in matters that do not violate Sharia; sinful commands must be denounced.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the dilemma of obeying versus denouncing an Imam who sins?
A) By saying the Imam is infallible and cannot sin
B) By stating that denunciation is never obligatory
C) By limiting obedience to lawful matters and requiring denunciation of sin
D) By arguing that the verse of obedience is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The duty to obey authority is limited to lawful commands; sin requires denunciation, not obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a person in authority—a parent, employer, or leader—doing something wrong, do you feel torn between loyalty and truth? How does this lesson refine your sense of duty? Will you now take a concrete step to speak out or at least not internally justify the wrong by 'obedience'?
?
Have you ever used the excuse of 'staying quiet to keep the peace' to avoid confronting a sin? What in this passage strips that excuse of its legitimacy? What would it cost you to act on denunciation next time?
?
If you truly believed that Allah commands denunciation of sin even from those you respect, what would change about your daily interactions? Write one specific situation you will handle differently from today.

The Objection of Infallible Transmission Refuted
Sanusi refutes the need for an infallible transmitter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the transmission of Sharia does not require an infallible Imam and how the community's collective protection suffices.

Core Idea
The community's collective transmission is sufficient; no infallible Imam is needed.

Sanusi presents the seventh objection: that Sharia must be transmitted by an infallible Imam. He refutes it by showing that conjecture (ẓann) suffices for non-definitive rulings, and the definitive ones are transmitted by tawātur or the entire community, which is collectively protected from error. He then challenges the Shia to explain how their own Sharia reached them from an Imam who is only a name.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated: Sharia needs a transmitter, and since tawātur is not available for every ruling, only an infallible Imam can suffice.
2
Study the answer
Read the answer: conjecture is sufficient for some matters, so single reports from trustworthy narrators are acceptable. Definitive matters rely on tawātur or the entire community.
Note the distinction between ẓann (conjecture) and qaṭ‘ī (definitive).
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Consider Sanusi's closing question: how did the Shia receive their Sharia from an Imam who is merely a name? This exposes a contradiction in their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أنه لا بد للشريعة من ناقل ولا يوجد في كل حكم حكم أهل التواتر معنعناً إلى انقراض العصر. فلم يبق إلا أن يكون إماماً معصوماً عن الخطأ. والجواب أن الظن كاف في البعض، فينقل بطريق الآحاد من الثقاة . وأما القطعي فإلى أهل التواتر أو جميع الأمة ، وهم أهل عصمة عن الخطأ، فلا حاجة إلى معصوم بالمعنى الذي قصد . ثم وليت شعري - بأي طريق نقلت الشريعة إلى الشيعة من الإمام الذي لا يوجد منه إلا الاسم.
The seventh: That the Sharī‘ah must have a transmitter, and the report of the people of tawātur is not found in every ruling transmitted continuously (mu‘an‘anan) until the end of the age. So nothing remains except that the Imam be infallible from error.
The answer: That conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient in some matters, so it is transmitted by the way of the single reports from trustworthy narrators. As for the definitive (qaṭ‘ī), it is transmitted by the people of tawātur or the entire community, and they are people of protection from error, so there is no need for an infallible in the sense intended. Then, woe is me—by which path was the Sharī‘ah transmitted to the Shī‘ah from an Imam who is nothing but a name?
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection and its refutation. The objector argues that the Sharī‘ah requires a transmitter, and since not every ruling is transmitted by tawātur, the only solution is an infallible Imam. The response states that conjecture (ẓann) suffices for some matters, so transmission occurs via single reports (āḥād) from trustworthy narrators. Definitive matters are transmitted by tawātur or the entire community, which is collectively protected from error. Thus, no infallible Imam in the claimed sense is needed. The author then rhetorically asks how the Shī‘ah themselves received the Sharī‘ah from an Imam who is only a name.

Key Terms
ناقل
تواتر
آحاد
ظن
قطعي
عصمة
ثقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sharia transmission requires an individual infallible Imam at all times.
Correction: The answer shows that the community collectively is protected from error (like the concept of ijmā‘), and conjectural rulings suffice via trustworthy narrators.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the seventh objection, how are definitive (qaṭ‘ī) rulings transmitted?
A) Through a single infallible Imam
B) Through the people of tawātur or the entire community
C) Through single reports from trustworthy narrators
D) Through dreams and inspirations

Key Takeaway
The transmission of Sharia does not require an infallible Imam; conjecture suffices for some matters and the community is collectively protected for definitive ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever demand absolute certainty before acting on a religious command? How does Sanusi's acceptance of conjecture (ẓann) in some matters challenge your tendency to doubt or delay obedience?
?
Sanusi argues that the community itself is protected from error collectively. Have you ever distrusted the consensus of scholars or the mainstream understanding of Islam? What would it take for you to submit to the collective guidance of the Ummah rather than your own individual reasoning?
?
The rhetorical question reveals that the Shia cannot even explain how their own Sharia reached them from a hidden Imam. Where in your faith do you rely on an inaccessible or invisible authority rather than the tangible transmission of the Qur'an and Sunnah through the trustworthy scholars of this Ummah?

Refutation of Extravagant Conditions for the Imam
The author dismisses Rafidah stipulations of miracles and supernatural knowledge for the Imam as superstitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why stipulating miracles and supernatural knowledge for the Imam is considered superstitious and leads to the denial of the Imamate and Sharia.

Core Idea
Extravagant conditions for the Imam undermine the Imamate and Sharia.

This lesson examines a passage from al-Maqasid that refutes the Rafidah's extreme conditions for the Imam. The author argues that requiring miracles and knowledge of the unseen is superstitious and leads to the denial of the Imamate itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the author's main point.
2
Identify the stipulated conditions
List the conditions that the Rafidah extremists require for the Imam (miracles, knowledge of unseen, languages, etc.).
3
Understand why they are called superstitions
The author labels these conditions 'khurāfāt' (superstitions). Consider why such requirements are unrealistic and excessive.
4
Grasp the harmful consequence
The author warns that these stipulations lead to denying the Imam and rejecting the Sharia. Reflect on how this could happen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما اشتراط المعجزة والعلم بالمغيبات واللغات والحرف والصناعات وطبائع الأغذية والأدوية وعجائب البر والبحر والسماء والأرض فمن الخرافات). قد اشترط الغلاة من الروافض أن يكون الإمام صاحب معجزة عالماً بالغيوب، اللغات، وبجميع الحرف والصناعات وبطبائع الأغذية والأدوية و بعجائب البر والبحر والسماء والأرض. وهذه خرافات مفضية إلى نفي الإمام ورفض الشريعة والأحكام .
He said: As for stipulating miracles, knowledge of the unseen, languages, crafts and industries, natures of foods and medicines, wonders of the land, sea, sky, and earth—these are superstitions. The extremists among the Rāfiḍah have stipulated that the Imam be a miracle-worker, knower of the unseen, languages, all crafts and industries, natures of foods and medicines, wonders of the land, sea, sky, and earth. These are superstitions leading to the denial of the Imam and rejection of the Sharī‘ah and rulings.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage quotes the original author (likely the matn of al-Maqasid) and then comments on it. It declares that stipulating miracles, knowledge of the unseen, languages, crafts, industries, properties of foods and medicines, and wonders of creation for the Imam is superstitious. The commentator identifies the ‘ghulāt’ (extremists) among the Rāfiḍah as holding these conditions. He asserts that such stipulations lead to the denial of the Imam and rejection of the Sharī‘ah and its rulings.

Key Terms
معجزة
غيوب
لغات
حرف
صناعات
خرافات
رفض
غلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam must possess supernatural abilities like performing miracles and knowing all unseen things.
Correction: The author argues that such stipulations are superstitious and lead to the denial of the Imam and rejection of Sharia.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the stipulation of miracles and knowledge of the unseen for the Imam a superstition (khurāfāt)?
A) Because these are impossible for any human being
B) Because they lead to the denial of the Imam and rejection of Sharia
C) Because the Quran explicitly forbids such conditions
D) Because the Sunnah proves that the early caliphs lacked these

Key Takeaway
Stipulating miracles and supernatural knowledge for the Imam is superstitious and leads to the denial of the Imam and Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected a religious leader to be infallible or superhuman? How does this lesson's warning against such expectations challenge your own assumptions?
?
In what area of your life do you demand perfection from others that you would never demand from yourself, and how might that lead you to reject the good that is present?
?
If you were to remove all unrealistic expectations from your view of the Imamate or religious authority, what would remain that is truly required, and are you living up to that?

The Sixth Objection: Sinning Imam
Al-Taftazani presents the sixth objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth Shi'a objection regarding the sinning imam and the Sunni response that obedience is conditional upon Shari'a compliance.

Core Idea
Obedience to the imam is conditional on Shari'a compliance.

This passage addresses one of the key Shi'a objections to the Sunni position on imamate. The objector raises a dilemma about obeying a sinning imam. Al-Taftazani presents the objection and then provides a clear answer that resolves the apparent contradiction by limiting the scope of obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection presents two alternatives: either it is obligatory to prevent the sinning imam, which contradicts the obligation to obey him; or it is not obligatory, which contradicts text and consensus. Find these two horns.
3
Understand the answer
The answer clarifies that obedience is only required in matters that do not contradict the Shari'a. If the imam's action violates it, then prevention is obligatory, or silence if necessary.
4
Reflect on the key principle
Notice how this response avoids the logical contradiction by qualifying the scope of obedience. This is a central principle in Sunni political theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - أنه لو أقدم على المعصية، فإما أن يجب الإنكار عليه وهو مضاد لوجوب إطاعته الثابت بقوله تعالى أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم مام کرد فيلزم اجتماع الضدين، وإما أن لا يجب وهو خلاف النص والإجماع . والجواب أن وجوب الطاعة إنما هو فيما لا يخالف الشرع . وأما فيما يخالفه فالرد والإنكار ، وإن لم يتيسر فسكوت عن اضطرار.
The sixth: That if he commits disobedience, then either it is obligatory to prevent him, and that opposes the obligation of obeying him established by His saying 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' [4:59] '?' thus two opposites will coincide; or it is not obligatory, and that is contrary to the text and consensus. The answer: the obligation of obedience is only in what does not contradict the Shari'a. As for what contradicts it, then it is rejection and prevention; and if that is not possible, then silence out of necessity.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection of the Rafida (Shi'a) against the Sunni position that imamate is established by choice. The objector argues: if the imam commits a sin, then either it is obligatory to forbid his action (enjoin right), but that would contradict the obligation to obey him (which is proven by the verse 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you'), leading to a logical contradiction; or it is not obligatory to forbid him, which contradicts both the Qur'an and consensus. The answer clarifies that the obligation to obey the imam is only in matters that do not contradict the Shari'a. If the imam's action violates the Shari'a, then it is obligatory to forbid and oppose him; but if one is unable to do so without greater harm, then silence is permitted due to necessity.

Key Terms
المعصية
الإنكار
وجوب الطاعة
الضدين
النص
الإجماع
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obedience to the imam is absolute and required even if he commits a sin.
Correction: Obedience is only in what does not contradict the Shari'a. If the imam sins, it is obligatory to forbid him, or at least remain silent out of necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, when is obedience to the imam not required?
A) When it causes fitna.
B) When the imam commits a sin.
C) When the imam is not infallible.
D) When it contradicts the Shari'a.

Key Takeaway
Obedience to the imam is limited to what does not violate the Shari'a; sinning imams must be opposed or silently tolerated out of necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader or person in authority doing something wrong, do you feel the weight of responsibility to speak up, or do you stay silent out of comfort? What does this passage teach you about the condition of your own obedience?
?
Have you ever justified obeying a command that troubled your conscience by telling yourself 'I have to obey'? How does Allah's verse limiting obedience to what does not contradict Shari'a challenge your excuses?
?
If you are unable to stop an injustice, does your silence come from genuine necessity or from fear of losing status? Ask yourself: would you be willing to oppose even if it cost you something valuable?

The Need for an Infallible Transmitter
Al-Taftazani presents the seventh Shi'a objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a objection that an infallible imam is needed to transmit Shari'a rulings and how the Sunni response shows that conjecture or collective infallibility suffices.

Core Idea
An infallible imam is not required; the community or reliable transmitters suffice.

Al-Taftazani addresses the seventh objection of the Rafida, who argue that without an infallible imam, no ruling can be reliably transmitted. This objection challenges the Sunni reliance on transmitted knowledge. The response distinguishes between matters requiring certainty and those where conjecture is acceptable, showing that the entire community can be a source of infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify the objection's core claim
The Shi'a claim that since not every ruling has continuous mass transmission, an infallible imam is necessary. Find this in the first sentence.
The term 'ناقل' is key.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer distinguishes between rulings where conjecture (ظن) is sufficient and those requiring certainty (قطعي). For each, it provides a transmitter: trustworthy individuals or the community/tawatur.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Al-Taftazani ends with a challenge: how did the Shi'a receive their Shari'a from an imam who is only a name? Consider the implications.
This question undermines the Shi'a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أنه لا بد للشريعة من ناقل ولا يوجد في كل حكم حكم أهل التواتر معنعناً إلى انقراض العصر. فلم يبق إلا أن يكون إماماً معصوماً عن الخطأ. والجواب أن الظن كاف في البعض، فينقل بطريق الآحاد من الثقاة . وأما القطعي فإلى أهل التواتر أو جميع الأمة ، وهم أهل عصمة عن الخطأ، فلا حاجة إلى معصوم بالمعنى الذي قصد . ثم وليت شعري - بأي طريق نقلت الشريعة إلى الشيعة من الإمام الذي لا يوجد منه إلا الاسم.
The seventh: That the Shari'a must have a transmitter, and there does not exist in every ruling the verdict of the people of tawatur (mass transmission) handed down continuously until the end of the age. So nothing remains except that there be an imam infallible from error. The answer: conjecture is sufficient in some matters, so it is transmitted through the reports of trustworthy individuals. As for certain knowledge, it is from the people of tawatur or the entire community, and they are the people of infallibility from error, so there is no need for an infallible in the sense intended. Then I wonder—by what means was the Shari'a transmitted to the Shi'a from the imam from whom nothing exists except the name?
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection of the Rafida. They argue that the Shari'a requires a transmitter (nāqil) for its rulings, and not every ruling has a continuous chain of transmission (tawātur) spanning the entire period. Therefore, there must be an infallible imam to convey the rulings. The answer distinguishes between two types of rulings: for some, conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient, and these can be transmitted through trustworthy individuals (āḥād). For certain knowledge (qaṭ'ī), it comes from the people of tawatur or the consensus of the entire community, who are collectively protected from error. Thus, there is no need for an individual infallible imam. The passage ends with a rhetorical question: how was the Shari'a transmitted to the Shi'a when their imam is only a name?

أهل التواتر
معنعناً
انقراض العصر
ظن
آحاد
ثقاة
قطعي
جميع الأمة
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every transmitted ruling requires an infallible imam.
Correction: The answer shows that conjecture suffices for some rulings and tawatur or consensus for others, so no individual infallible is needed.

Checkpoint
How does the answer address the need for an infallible transmitter?
A) By asserting that the entire community is infallible.
B) By stating that conjecture is never sufficient.
C) By claiming that all rulings are transmitted via tawatur.
D) By denying the need for any transmitter.

Key Takeaway
The community's consensus or mass transmission suffices for certainty; conjecture from trustworthy individuals suffices for non-certain matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek religious knowledge, do you ever feel the need for an 'infallible' teacher, or are you willing to trust the collective transmission of the ummah? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on human authority versus communal scholarship? Commit to verifying one belief you hold this week by checking its transmission chain.
?
Al-Taftazani says conjecture is sufficient for many rulings. Are you comfortable living with uncertainty in your faith, or do you insist on certainty in every matter? How does that demand affect your peace of mind and your practice? Choose one area where you can accept conjecture and act on a trustworthy opinion without anxiety.
?
The passage ends with a stinging question: how did the Shi'a receive their Shari'a from an imam who is only a name? Turn that question on yourself: Could you trace your own religious knowledge back through reliable transmitters, or do you follow authorities without knowing their chains? What is one step you can take this week to strengthen your connection to the authentic sources?

Refuting Extreme Conditions for the Imam
Al-Taftazani dismisses extravagant Shi'a conditions for the imam as absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni scholars reject the extreme conditions placed by some Shi'a on the imam, and how such conditions undermine the institution of imamate.

Core Idea
Extreme conditions for the imam are self-defeating superstitions.

Al-Taftazani here confronts a set of highly extravagant conditions that some extremist Shi'a groups have placed on the imam. He dismisses them as mere superstitions, arguing that if such conditions were required, no one could ever be imam, effectively nullifying the institution itself. This lesson unpacks his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of conditions and the strong dismissal.
The passage begins with a quotation from the matn.
2
Identify the conditions and their proponents
List the conditions mentioned: miracles, knowledge of unseen, languages, crafts, etc. Note that al-Taftazani attributes these to the 'extremists among the Rawafid'.
3
Understand the critique
Why are these called 'superstitions'? Reflect on the reasoning: they are impossible to fulfill, so requiring them leads to negating the imam altogether.
The consequence is not just rejection of these conditions but rejection of the Shari'a itself.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument protects the practical and realistic nature of the imamate in Sunni theology.
This is a polemical point against extreme Shi'a positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما اشتراط : (وأما اشتراط المعجزة والعلم بالمغيبات واللغات والحرف والصناعات وطبائع الأغذية والأدوية وعجائب البر والبحر والسماء والأرض فمن الخرافات). قد اشترط الغلاة من الروافض أن يكون الإمام صاحب معجزة عالماً بالغيوب، اللغات، وبجميع الحرف والصناعات وبطبائع الأغذية والأدوية و بعجائب البر والبحر والسماء والأرض. وهذه خرافات مفضية إلى نفي الإمام ورفض الشريعة والأحكام .
He said: As for the condition—And as for stipulating miracles and knowledge of unseen things, languages, crafts, industries, natures of foods and medicines, and wonders of land, sea, sky, and earth—these are superstitions. The extremists among the Rawafid have stipulated that the imam be one who performs a miracle, knows the unseen, languages, and all crafts and industries, and the natures of foods and medicines, and the wonders of land, sea, sky, and earth. These are superstitions leading to the negation of the imam and the rejection of the Shari'a and its rulings.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage consists of a heading and then explanation. Al-Taftazani quotes the previous author (likely al-Dawwani or the matn) stating that requiring miracles and comprehensive knowledge of all things is superstitious. He then elaborates: the extremist Rafida (Shi'a) have made these conditions, but they are baseless superstitions that would lead to denying the institution of imamate itself and rejecting the Shari'a. The implication is that such conditions are impossible to fulfill, so if they were required, no one would qualify, thus nullifying the imamate.

Key Terms

معجزة
الغلاة
الروافض
خرافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sunni scholars deny the possibility of miracles or prophetic knowledge.
Correction: The passage only rejects these as necessary conditions for the imam, not as possibilities. Miracles and knowledge of the unseen are possible for prophets, but requiring them of an imam is unrealistic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do these superstitious conditions ultimately lead to?
A) The imam having more knowledge
B) The negation of the imam and rejection of Shari'a
C) The acceptance of the imam by all Muslims
D) The strengthening of the imamate

Key Takeaway
Impossible conditions for the imam are self-defeating; they nullify the imamate and undermine the Shari'a.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might that attitude, when generalized, lead to rejecting the entire system of Shari'a, as Al-Taftazani warns? What would it take for you to accept a leader with realistic expectations?
?
Al-Taftazani calls these extreme conditions 'superstitions' that lead to denying the imamate. What unrealistic expectations do you hold that might cause you to dismiss sound guidance from scholars or those in authority? Can you identify one such expectation that you need to let go of, and how will you work to change it?
?
The Rawafid's conditions were so extreme that they effectively nullified the imamate entirely. In your own life, do you ever set impossible standards for others (family, friends, community members) that make it impossible to work with them? How can you adopt a more balanced approach based on what is actually necessary, rather than what is ideal?

The Sunni Basis for Imamate by Choice
Al-Taftazani states the Sunni view on the establishment of imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni evidence for establishing imamate through the choice of the people of binding and loosing, based on consensus and prophetic precedent.

Core Idea
Imamate is established by community choice, not divine text.

Al-Taftazani opens the third section by stating the majority Sunni position on how the imamate is established: through the selection of the qualified representatives of the community, known as ahl al-hall wal-'aqd. He draws on two key historical precedents to support this view: the consensus on Abu Bakr's succession and the Companions' practice after the Prophet and after Uthman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Sunni claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how al-Taftazani begins by affirming that imamate is established by choice (ikhtiyār) of ahl al-hall wal-'aqd.
The phrase 'even if they are few' is significant.
2
Examine the first evidence: consensus on Abu Bakr
The first evidence is the consensus (ijmāʿ) on the imamate of Abu Bakr, which occurred without explicit textual designation (naṣṣ) and without waiting for universal agreement.
This shows that the selection process did not require a direct command from Allah.
3
Examine the second evidence: practice of the Companions
The second evidence is the Companions' engagement in bayʿa (oath of allegiance) and choice after the Prophet and after Uthman, without 'takbir' (likely meaning without delay or without a formal proclamation).
This precedent reinforces the legitimacy of choice-based succession.
4
Reflect on the role of ahl al-hall wal-'aqd
Consider why the community's representatives are qualified to choose the imam, and why their decision is binding even if they are few.
This connects to the broader Sunni theory of collective authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث - الإمامة تثبت عند أكثر الفرق باختيار أهل الحل والعقد وإن قلوا للإجماع على إمامة أبي بكر من غير نص ولا توقف إلى اتفاق الكل. وعلى اشتغال الصحابة بعد النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وبعد عثمان (رضي الله تعالى عنه) بالبيعة والاختيار من غير تكبير وخالفت الشيعة بوجوه : الأول - أن من الشروط مالا يعلمه أهل البيعة كالعصمة والأفضلية، والعلم بالدين كله .قلنا لو سلم الاشتراط فالظن كاف.
The third section: The imamate is established, according to most sects, by the choice of the people of binding and loosing, even if they are few, based on consensus on the imamate of Abu Bakr without [explicit] text and without awaiting agreement of all. And based on the Companions' engagement after the Prophet (peace be upon him) and after Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) in the oath of allegiance and choice without 'takbir'. And the Shi'a opposed with aspects: First: That among the conditions are things not known to the people of the oath of allegiance, such as infallibility, superiority, and knowledge of the entire religion. We said: Even if the conditions are granted, conjecture is sufficient.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage opens the third section (mabḥath) on how imamate is established. Al-Taftazani states the Sunni position: imamate is established by the choice (ikhtiyār) of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-ḥall wal-ʿaqd), even if few, as evidenced by the consensus on Abu Bakr's succession without explicit textual designation (naṣṣ) and without waiting for universal agreement. Further evidence is the practice of the Companions after the Prophet and after Uthman, who gave allegiance (bayʿa) and chose without 'takbir' (unclear). Then he lists the first Shi'a objection: the conditions for imamate (like infallibility, superiority, and complete religious knowledge) are not known to those who choose. The answer: even if granted that these conditions are required, conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient, so the choice of the people of binding and loosing is valid.

Key Terms
أهل الحل والعقد
إجماع
نص
بيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni view requires an explicit text (naṣṣ) designating the imam.
Correction: The passage shows that the imamate of Abū Bakr was established by consensus without naṣṣ, proving that explicit designation is not a condition.

Checkpoint
What two pieces of evidence does al-Taftazani cite for the Sunni position that imamate is established by choice?
A) A verse from the Qur'an and a hadith.
B) Consensus on Abū Bakr's imamate and the practice of the Companions.
C) The practice of the Shi'a imams and the opinion of the majority.
D) The infallibility of the electors and the need for universal agreement.

Key Takeaway
Sunnis hold that imamate is legitimately established by the selection of qualified community representatives, as proven by early Islamic consensus and practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of leadership in your own community, do you naturally wait for a clear sign from Allah before accepting someone, or do you trust the collective wisdom of the righteous? How does the example of Abu Bakr's selection challenge your reliance on explicit signs over consensus?
?
The companions quickly gave bay'a without delay after the Prophet's death. Is there an area in your life where you delay committing to what is clearly beneficial because you are awaiting a 'text' or certainty? What would it look like to act on collective wisdom today?
?
The passage says imamate is established by 'choice' not 'text'. Do you seek divine signs for every decision, or do you trust the process of consultation? How can you balance reliance on Allah with human responsibility?

Answering the First Shi'a Objection
Al-Taftazani records the first Shi'a objection and gives the Sunni reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a objection that electors cannot know the necessary conditions for imamate, and the Sunni response that conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient.

Core Idea
Even if conditions like infallibility are required, probability is enough.

After stating the Sunni position, al-Taftazani records the first objection from the Shi'a: that the electors (ahl al-bayʿa) cannot know whether the candidate meets essential conditions like infallibility ('iṣma), superiority (afḍaliyya), and complete religious knowledge. The Sunni response is concise but profound: even if these conditions are accepted, conjecture (ẓann) suffices for the selection, as it does in many areas of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a objection carefully
Read the part of the passage where the Shi'a objection is presented: 'That among the conditions are things not known to the people of the oath of allegiance, such as...'
Note the three conditions listed: infallibility, superiority, and complete religious knowledge.
2
Understand the Sunni response
The response is: 'Even if the conditions are granted, conjecture is sufficient.' This is a legal principle that ẓann (dominant opinion) is a valid basis for action in Islamic jurisprudence.
The response does not deny the conditions but shifts the epistemological standard.
3
Connect to broader Islamic legal theory
Consider how ẓann is used in other areas of Islamic law, such as ijtihād and qiyās, to arrive at rulings. This shows that certainty is not always required.
This point reinforces the Sunni argument that the choice of the imam is valid even without absolute knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث - الإمامة تثبت عند أكثر الفرق باختيار أهل الحل والعقد وإن قلوا للإجماع على إمامة أبي بكر من غير نص ولا توقف إلى اتفاق الكل. وعلى اشتغال الصحابة بعد النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وبعد عثمان (رضي الله تعالى عنه) بالبيعة والاختيار من غير تكبير وخالفت الشيعة بوجوه : الأول - أن من الشروط مالا يعلمه أهل البيعة كالعصمة والأفضلية، والعلم بالدين كله .قلنا لو سلم الاشتراط فالظن كاف.
The third section: The imamate is established, according to most sects, by the choice of the people of binding and loosing, even if they are few, based on consensus on the imamate of Abu Bakr without [explicit] text and without awaiting agreement of all. And based on the Companions' engagement after the Prophet (peace be upon him) and after Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) in the oath of allegiance and choice without 'takbir'. And the Shi'a opposed with aspects: First: That among the conditions are things not known to the people of the oath of allegiance, such as infallibility, superiority, and knowledge of the entire religion. We said: Even if the conditions are granted, conjecture is sufficient.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage opens the third section (mabḥath) on how imamate is established. Al-Taftazani states the Sunni position: imamate is established by the choice (ikhtiyār) of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-ḥall wal-ʿaqd), even if few, as evidenced by the consensus on Abu Bakr's succession without explicit textual designation (naṣṣ) and without waiting for universal agreement. Further evidence is the practice of the Companions after the Prophet and after Uthman, who gave allegiance (bayʿa) and chose without 'takbir' (unclear). Then he lists the first Shi'a objection: the conditions for imamate (like infallibility, superiority, and complete religious knowledge) are not known to those who choose. The answer: even if granted that these conditions are required, conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient, so the choice of the people of binding and loosing is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni response to the Shi'a objection is to deny that the conditions are required.
Correction: The Sunni response does not deny the conditions; it accepts them but asserts that conjecture is sufficient, so the election remains valid.

Checkpoint

A) The conditions are not actually required.
B) Conjecture (ẓann) is sufficient.
C) The electors are infallible themselves.
D) Only infallibility is required, and it is known.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on imamate remains valid even if the electors cannot achieve certainty about the candidate's qualities, because conjecture (ẓann) is a sufficient basis for decision.

?

?

?
This lesson shows that conjecture is sufficient in matters of imamate. How does that affect your own willingness to follow a leader without absolute proof of their infallibility? What does that reveal about your reliance on God's arrangement?

The Authority to Appoint Lesser Officials
Al-Taftazani answers a Shi'a objection by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the Shi'a objection that the people of binding and loosing lack authority to appoint an imam by analogy to lesser appointments.

Core Idea
If they cannot appoint a judge, it is because an imam already exists.

Al-Taftazani now addresses a second objection from the Shi'a: if the people of binding and loosing cannot even appoint a judge or a market inspector, how can they appoint an imam? The answer is deceptively simple: those lesser appointments are made by an imam. When no imam exists, the people must have the authority to appoint one, or the entire system of appointment collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its answer
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the objection and the even more concise answer.
2
Identify the analogy
The objection draws an analogy from the inability to appoint judges and hisba officials to the inability to appoint an imam. Understand the a fortiori (all the more so) reasoning.
The term 'أولى' indicates the a fortiori argument.
3
See how the answer reverses the analogy
The answer grants the premise but points out that the inability to appoint lesser officials is due to the existence of an imam. Thus the analogy actually proves the need for the people's authority when there is no imam.
This is a classic example of turning the opponent's argument against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن ليس إليهم تولية مثل القضاء والاحتساب فهذا أولى. قلنا : لو سلم فلوجود الإمام.
Second: That it is not within their authority to appoint someone like a judge or a hisba official, so this [appointment of an imam] is more entitled [to be excluded]. We said: Even if granted, it is because of the existence of the imam [who can appoint them].
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shi'a objection and its answer. The objectors argue that the people of binding and loosing do not have the authority to appoint a judge or a hisba official (market inspector), so by analogy they certainly have no authority to appoint a greater figure like the imam. The answer turns the analogy around: if they cannot appoint these officials, it is because there is an imam in place who appoints them. But when there is no imam, they must have the authority to appoint one, otherwise the system would fail.

Key Terms
تولية
قضاء
احتساب
أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection provides a valid disanalogy proving the people cannot appoint an imam.
Correction: The objection actually assumes the existence of an imam who appoints judges etc., so it does not apply when no imam exists.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Shi'a objection in this passage?
A) The people of binding and loosing are not infallible.
B) They lack the authority to appoint lesser officials, so they cannot appoint an imam.
C) The imam must be appointed by God.
D) The people of binding and loosing are not qualified.

Key Takeaway
The people of binding and loosing must have the authority to appoint an imam, because otherwise the system of appointing lesser officials would lack a foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see problems in your community, do you wait for a leader to fix them, or do you take responsibility? Which approach does this lesson's reasoning demand of you, and why?
?
If you were in a situation without a legitimate leader, would you feel entitled to appoint one? What fears or hesitations might hold you back from doing what the argument suggests is necessary?
?
The Shi'a objection assumed the imam's existence to deny the people's authority. In your own life, do you ever assume that someone else will handle matters so that you can avoid responsibility? How can you apply the rebuttal's logic to your own heart?

Third Objection: Fear of Fitna
Al-Taftazani answers the third Shi'a objection concerning civil strife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fear of civil strife (fitna) does not invalidate the principle of choice (ikhtiyar) in selecting an imam, and how the response weighs the greater evil of anarchy.

Core Idea
The greater fitna is having no imam at all.

This passage addresses the third Shi'a objection: that allowing the community to choose an imam leads to civil strife, as happened between Ali and Muawiya. Al-Taftazani responds by clarifying the conditions under which choice is exercised and by comparing the potential fitna to the far worse fitna of anarchy. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the objection and the two-part answer.
2
Identify the two parts of the response
The answer has two moves: first, it restricts the discussion to qualified electors; second, it compares magnitudes of fitna.
3
Consider the implied premise
Note the condition 'if they submit to the truth'—the argument assumes good faith. Ask: what if they don't?
This is an idealization typical of kalam.
4
Reflect on the key tradeoff
The passage balances the risk of fitna from choice against the certainty of fitna without an imam. Why is the latter worse?
This is a comparative harm argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن في ذلك إثارة الفتنة كما في زمن علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه) ومعاوية . قلنا: الكلام فيما إذا أذعنوا للحق واعتبروا جهات الترجيح . ولوسلم، ففتنة عدم الإمام أضعاف ذلك، إذ التقدير عدم النص. وإلا فلا اختيار عليه .
Third: That in that [choice] there is incitement of fitna (tribulation) as in the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Muawiya. We said: The discussion is when they submit to the truth and consider the aspects of preponderance. And even if granted, the fitna of the absence of an imam is many times that, since the assumption is the absence of [explicit] text. Otherwise, there is no choice for him.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shi'a objection: allowing choice of an imam can lead to fitna (civil strife), as happened between Ali and Muawiya. The answer: the discussion concerns a situation where the electors submit to the truth and weigh the factors properly—if they do, there is no fitna. Even if fitna were possible, the fitna caused by having no imam at all is far worse, especially given the assumption that no explicit text (nass) from the Prophet exists for a specific imam. If such a text existed, there would be no need for choice.

Key Terms
فتنة
إذعان
ترجيح
نص
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any choice of imam inevitably leads to fitna.
Correction: The response shows that fitna only occurs when electors do not submit to truth, and even then, the fitna of anarchy is greater.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the claim that choice leads to fitna?
A) Fitna is inevitable.
B) The absence of an imam causes greater fitna.
C) Only the Shi'a cause fitna.
D) The imam prevents fitna.

Key Takeaway
The fear of fitna from choosing an imam is outweighed by the greater evil of having no imam, provided the electors act sincerely and weigh the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided making a difficult decision because you feared it would cause conflict, only to find that the resulting uncertainty or inaction caused even greater harm? In your life, where do you need to step into a messy process of choosing right over wrong, trusting that the fitna of inaction is worse?
?
When you face a situation where both options involve some form of fitna, do you weigh the options based on truth and evidence, or do you let fear of immediate conflict paralyze you? What would it look like for you to submit to the truth and consider the aspects of preponderance in a current dilemma?

The Caliph: From the People or From God?
Al-Taftazani responds to the objection that a chosen caliph is only from the people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni response grounds the caliph's authority in divine sanction via Shari'a, even when chosen by the community.

Core Idea
A chosen caliph is God's caliph because Shari'a confirms it.

This passage presents a key Shi'a objection to the Sunni view of caliphate: if the people choose, the caliph is their representative, not God's. Al-Taftazani's answer is concise but profound: the Shari'a itself provides the proof that the community's choice carries divine approval. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the brevity and precision of both objection and response.
2
Identify the core claim of the objection
The objection asserts a dichotomy: human choice vs. divine appointment. Understand why the Shi'a see election as merely human.
The key phrase is 'خليفة منهم لا من الله ورسوله'.
3
Analyze the answer's logic
See how the answer shifts the basis from human choice to divine law. The Shari'a provides a proof (دليل) that ratifies the community's choice.
Ask yourself: how does دليل الشرع change the status of the chosen person?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن مختار أهل البيعة يكون خليفة منهم لا من الله ورسوله . قلنا : قام دليل الشرع (۱) على أن من اختاروه فهو خليفة الله ورسوله .
Fourth: That the chosen one of the people of the oath of allegiance would be a caliph from them, not from Allah and His Messenger. We said: The proof of the Shari'a has established that whomever they choose is the caliph of Allah and His Messenger.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Shi'a objection: if the people choose the caliph, he is their representative, not God's or the Prophet's. The answer: the Shari'a itself (divine law) has provided evidence (dalīl) that the person chosen by the community is indeed the caliph of Allah and His Messenger. That is, the legitimacy comes from the Shari'a's ratification of the community's choice, not from the mere act of choosing.

Key Terms
خليفة
أهل البيعة
دليل الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni view makes the caliph merely a human appointee with no divine sanction.
Correction: The Sunni position is that the Shari'a provides a proof that the chosen caliph is divinely appointed as the caliph of Allah and His Messenger.

Checkpoint
How does the passage justify that a chosen caliph is from Allah?
A) Through a direct divine text appointing him.
B) Through the Shari'a's proof that the chosen one is God's caliph.
C) Through the consensus of the people.
D) Through the infallibility of the choosers.

Key Takeaway
The Shari'a's proof transforms a human election into a divine appointment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a leader in your community, do you see your obedience as ultimately to Allah, or merely to a human authority? How would your attitude change if you believed that leader is Allah's caliph, even if chosen by people?
?
The objection assumes human choice and divine appointment are opposites. In your own life, where do you struggle to see God's hand in human decisions? Is there an area where you distrust God's ability to guide earthly processes?
?
If you truly believed that the Shari'a transforms a mere election into a divine appointment, how would that affect the way you participate in choosing leaders? Would you be more careful, more hopeful, or more obedient? What one change can you make today?

Two Rival Appointments: Objection and Answer
Al-Taftazani answers the problem of simultaneous rival choices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth Shi'a objection regarding simultaneous rival appointments and how the Sunni response resolves it.

Core Idea
Simultaneous imam appointments are resolved by preponderance or a third choice.

This lesson examines a common Shi'a objection to Sunni imamate theory: what if two groups simultaneously appoint two different imams, and no one knows which came first? The Sunni answer shows that practical solutions exist without leaving the community without a leader or falling into corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Shi'a hypothetical: two groups each contract for a candidate, precedence unknown. Understand the dilemma.
Note the four impossibilities listed.
2
Identify the dilemma
Why does the critic think the time would be empty of an imam? Because validating both, invalidating both, choosing one, or appointing a third are all ruled out.
3
Study the answer
Al-Taftazani offers two solutions: give preponderance to one or appoint a third candidate. See how each avoids corruption.
The claim 'no corruption' is key.
4
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why preponderance or a third appointment is permissible. Consider that the absence of an imam (interregnum) is the greater harm, so these solutions are necessary.
5
Connect to broader theology
This answer defends the possibility of a valid imamate even in disputed circumstances, upholding the Sunni view that the community can always produce a legitimate leader.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - إذا عقد أهلان لأهلين ولم يعلم السبق ، لزم خلو الزمان (۱) عن الإمام إذ لا سبيل إلى تصحيحهما ولا إبطالهما ولا تعيين الصحيح منهما، ولا نصب ثالث . قلنا : بل يرجح أحدهما أو ينصب ثالث، ولا فساد .
Fifth: If two groups make a contract for two [candidates] and the precedence is not known, then necessarily the time period would be empty of an imam, since there is no way to validate both, nor to invalidate both, nor to determine which is correct, nor to appoint a third. We said: Rather, one of them is given preponderance or a third is appointed, and there is no corruption.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth Shi'a objection: if two different groups of electors each appoint a different imam simultaneously, and it is unknown which occurred first, then the entire period would have no valid imam because we cannot accept both, reject both, choose between them, or appoint a third. The answer: we can either preponderate one over the other (tarjīḥ) by investigating which appointment was more correct, or we can appoint a third candidate, and this does not lead to corruption. Thus the objection is answered.

Key Terms
عقد
سبق
خلو الزمان
تصحيح
إبطال
ترجيح
نصب ثالث
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simultaneous rival appointments necessarily lead to an interregnum (time without an imam) with no possible resolution.
Correction: The Sunni answer shows that preponderance (tarjīḥ) or appointing a third candidate are valid solutions that prevent any interregnum and avoid corruption.

Checkpoint
What solutions does the answer offer for two rival appointments?
A) Invoke a new election immediately.
B) Preponderate one or appoint a third.
C) Accept both appointments.
D) Reject both and wait for consensus.

Key Takeaway
The fifth Shi'a objection is answered by showing that preponderance or a third appointment can resolve rival claims without corruption, preserving the continuity of the imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between two seemingly valid options in your life, do you tend to avoid deciding, leaving a 'vacuum'? How does al-Taftazani's practical answer challenge your hesitation and call you to act justly?
?
In your own spiritual community, are there unresolved disagreements that you have assumed are impossible to resolve? What would it mean for you to seek 'preponderance' or a 'third way' instead of accepting a leadership vacuum?
?
The objection assumed impossibility, but the answer revealed overlooked solutions. What difficulty in your relationship with Allah or others have you deemed unsolvable because you failed to see the simple steps of tarjih or nasb thalith? Will you take one concrete step today to break the deadlock?

Sixth Objection: Prophet's Succession and the Definition of Time
Al-Taftazani answers the objection and defines the term 'time'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth Shi'a objection regarding the Prophet's appointment of a successor and the Sunni response that absence of explicit appointment does not imply prohibition. You will also learn the philosophers' and theologians' definitions of time.

Core Idea
The Prophet's silence on a successor does not forbid establishing one through other means.

This lesson addresses the final objection in a series: the Prophet always appointed a successor for short absences, but did not do so before his death. The response argues that his silence does not prove forbiddance. Additionally, a footnote explains the concept of time, a key term in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the Shi'a claim and al-Taftazani's response.
The response uses the principle that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the premise of the objection and how the answer refutes it. Notice the key phrase: 'leaving something does not entail its prohibition.'
This is a common logical fallacy.
3
Understand the definition of time
Read the footnote defining time from two perspectives: philosophers (measure of celestial motion) and theologians (a known continuous quantity by which the hypothetical is measured).
This definition is a standard kalām concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - أنه (صلى الله عليه وسلم) لم يكن ترك الاستخلاف في أدنى غيبة، ولا البيان في أدنى ما (۱) يفتقر إلى البيان. قلنا : ذلك في الجملة، ولو سلم فلم يجزم بأنه لم يترك، مع أن ترك شيء لا يستلزم تحريمه. (۲) الزمان هو مقدار حركة الفلك الأطلس عند الحكماء وعند المتكلمين عبارة عن متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد آخر موهوم كما يقال آتيك عند طلوع الشمس فإن طلوع الشمس معلوم ومجيئه موهوم فإذا قرن ذلك الموهوم بذلك المعلوم زال الإيهام.
Sixth: That he (peace be upon him) did not neglect to appoint a successor in the briefest absence, nor [neglect] clarification in the least matter that requires clarification. We said: That is in general; and even if granted, it is not certain that he did not leave [an explicit appointment], and leaving something does not entail its prohibition. (2) Time is, according to philosophers, the measure of the motion of the outer sphere, and according to theologians, it is an expression of a known thing that changes by which another hypothetical changing thing is measured, as one says 'I will come to you at sunrise'; the sunrise is known and the coming is hypothetical, so when the hypothetical is paired with the known, the uncertainty is removed.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final Shi'a objection: the Prophet never failed to appoint a successor during even the shortest absence, nor to clarify any matter requiring clarification. Since he left this world without clear designation, this suggests no succession was intended. The answer: this claim is only true in a general sense; even if granted, it does not prove that he did not leave a designation—absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. Moreover, if he did not leave an explicit text, that does not mean it is forbidden to establish an imam through other means. The passage then includes a footnote defining time (zamān): philosophers define it as the measure of the motion of the outer sphere (falak al-aṭlas), while theologians define it as a known continuous quantity by which another hypothetical quantity is measured, as in 'I will come at sunrise'—the sunrise is known, my coming is hypothetical; pairing them removes uncertainty.

Key Terms
استخلاف
غيبة
بيان
تحريم
الزمان
الفلك الأطلس
موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the Prophet did not explicitly appoint a successor, then appointing one is forbidden.
Correction: The passage clarifies that leaving something does not entail its prohibition; absence of explicit appointment does not make it forbidden to choose a leader through other means.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to this objection?
A) The Prophet did leave a successor.
B) The absence of an explicit appointment does not prove prohibition.
C) The Prophet was unable to appoint a successor.
D) The Shi'a misinterpret the Prophet's actions.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's failure to explicitly appoint a successor does not make it forbidden to choose one, and time is defined differently by philosophers (measure of motion) and theologians (a known continuous quantity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an important decision for which you have no clear command from Allah, do you assume it is forbidden or that you have freedom to choose? What does that assumption say about your trust in His guidance?
?
The theologians define time as a known quantity that removes uncertainty about hypothetical events. In your daily life, what 'known' anchors do you rely on—like prayer times or the sun's movement—to bring certainty to your uncertain plans? Are you as consistent in using divine commands to guide your choices?
?
The Prophet's supposed silence on succession does not make it forbidden to choose a leader. Is there any matter in your life where you have used Allah's silence as an excuse to ignore a duty? How can you ensure you are not confusing silence with permissibility?

Introduction and First Two Objections
Lists first two Shi'a objections and Sunni responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the first two Shi'a objections regarding the conditions for imamate and the authority of the community.

Core Idea
The community's choice suffices even if conditions are not fully known.

The passage begins by noting the Companions' practice of selecting a caliph through bay'ah and choice. The Shi'a raised objections against this process. In this lesson, we examine the first two objections: that the electors do not know all necessary conditions (like infallibility) and that they are not authorized even for minor appointments, so how for the imamate? The Sunni responses are concise: ẓann (reasonable assumption) suffices, and the imam's existence justifies the appointment.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening and first two objections
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the second response. Notice the structure: objection then 'قلنا' response.
The first objection lists conditions like infallibility and full knowledge.
2
Understand the first objection and response
The Shi'a argue that the people of allegiance (ahl al-bay'ah) don't know all required conditions. The Sunni response is that if conditions are required, ẓann (reasonable assumption) is sufficient. Consider why ẓann is acceptable in this context.
This relates to the principle of al-ẓann al-kāfī.
3
Understand the second objection and response
The second objection argues that if the community cannot appoint minor officials, how can they appoint an imam? The response: because the imam exists (li-wujūd al-imām). Reflect on the logic: the existence of an imam validates the appointment process.
4
Connect to the broader context
These objections challenge the legitimacy of the caliphate. Consider how these responses establish a foundation for the Sunni position on communal choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتغال الصحابة بعد النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) وبعد عثمان (رضي الله تعالى عنه) بالبيعة والاختيار من غير تكبير وخالفت الشيعة بوجوه :
الأول - أن من الشروط مالا يعلمه أهل البيعة كالعصمة والأفضلية، والعلم بالدين كله .
قلنا لو سلم الاشتراط فالظن كاف.
الثاني - أن ليس إليهم تولية مثل القضاء والاحتساب فهذا أولى.
قلنا : لو سلم فلوجود الإمام.
الثالث - أن في ذلك إثارة الفتنة كما في زمن علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه) ومعاوية .
قلنا: الكلام فيما إذا أذعنوا للحق واعتبروا جهات الترجيح . ولوسلم، ففتنة عدم الإمام أضعاف ذلك، إذ التقدير عدم النص. وإلا فلا اختيار عليه .
الرابع - أن مختار أهل البيعة يكون خليفة منهم لا من الله ورسوله . قلنا : قام دليل الشرع على أن من اختاروه فهو خليفة الله ورسوله .
الخامس - إذا عقد أهلان لأهلين ولم يعلم السبق ، لزم خلو الزمان عن الإمام إذ لا سبيل إلى تصحيحهما ولا إبطالهما ولا تعيين الصحيح منهما، ولا نصب ثالث .
قلنا : بل يرجح أحدهما أو ينصب ثالث، ولا فساد .
السادس - أنه (صلى الله عليه وسلم) لم يكن ترك الاستخلاف في أدنى غيبة، ولا البيان في أدنى ما يحتاج إليه، فكيف في غيبة الوفاة وفي أساس المهمات .
السابع - أن النبي ( أرأف بأمته من الأب لولده، فكيف ترك الوصية لهم إلى أحد ؟
قلنا : التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال .
الثامن - قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم والإمامة من معظمات أمر الدين، فكيف تهمل ؟
قلنا : التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال . )
The engagement of the Companions after the Prophet (peace be upon him) and after Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) in the pledge of allegiance and selection without takbir [؟] and the Shi'a opposed in aspects:
First - that among the conditions are things not known to the people of allegiance, such as infallibility and superiority, and knowledge of the entire religion.
We said: if the condition is granted, then assumption (al-ẓann) is sufficient.
Second - that they are not authorized to appoint positions like judge and market inspector, so this [imamate] is more appropriate.
We said: if granted, then because the imam exists.
Third - that this stirs up sedition (al-fitna) as in the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Muawiya.
We said: the discussion is when they submit to the truth and consider the aspects of preponderance. And if granted, the sedition of lack of an imam is greater than that, since the assumption is the absence of a text. Otherwise, there is no choice in the matter.
Fourth - that the chosen one of the people of allegiance becomes a caliph from them, not from Allah and His Messenger. We said: the evidence of the Law (al-sharʿ) has established that whomever they choose is the caliph of Allah and His Messenger.
Fifth - if two groups contract [allegiance] for two persons and the precedence is not known, then a period void of an imam becomes necessary, because there is no way to validate both, nor to invalidate both, nor to determine which is correct, nor to appoint a third.
We said: rather, one of them is given preponderance or a third is appointed, and there is no corruption.
Sixth - that he (peace be upon him) did not neglect to appoint a successor in the slightest absence, nor to explain the smallest thing needed, so how [could he neglect] in the absence of death and in the foundation of important matters?
Seventh - that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is more compassionate to his community than a father to his child, so how did he leave the bequest for them to anyone?
We said: delegation to their choice and their independent judgment is a kind of succession, bequest, and completion.
Eighth - His statement, Exalted: Today I have perfected for you your religion, and imamate is among the greatest matters of religion, so how is it neglected? We said: delegation to their choice and their independent judgment is a kind of succession, bequest, and completion.)
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents eight objections raised by the Shi'a against the legitimacy of the caliphate as established by the Companions, and provides Sunni refutations for each. The author lists them in sequence. 
First objection: The people of allegiance do not know all the required conditions for an imam (like infallibility, superiority, full religious knowledge). Response: Even if such conditions are required, a reasonable assumption (ẓann) suffices.
Second: The people of allegiance are not even entrusted with appointing minor officials, so how can they appoint an imam? Response: They can, because appointing an imam is even more important, and the imam exists to authorize them.
Third: The process of election caused sedition, as in the time of Ali and Muawiya. Response: Sedition occurs only when people do not submit to truth and ignore proper considerations. Moreover, the sedition of having no imam is far worse.
Fourth: The elected caliph is chosen by men, not by God. Response: The Law (sharʿ) declares that whomever the community chooses is God's and His Messenger's caliph.
Fifth: If two rival contracts occur, it leads to a period without an imam. Response: One is preferenced or a third is appointed; no corruption follows.
Sixth: The Prophet did not neglect to appoint a successor even for short absences, so why would he for death? Response: (Missing in OCR.)
Seventh: The Prophet is more compassionate than a father, so would he leave no will regarding leadership? Response: Delegating to the community's choice and ijtihad is itself a form of succession and bequest.
Eighth: Verse 'Today I perfected your religion' implies that imamate, being a major matter, would not be neglected. Response: Same as seventh: delegation is perfection.

Key Terms
بيعة
إمامة
ظن
استخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, what suffices if the conditions for imamate are required?
A) Certain knowledge (yaqīn)
B) Reasonable assumption (ẓann)
C) Divine revelation (waḥy)
D) Consensus of scholars (ijmāʿ)

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position holds that the community's ẓann-based choice is sufficient and that the imam's existence validates the election process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision where you cannot be absolutely certain, do you still act on reasonable assumption, or do you paralyze yourself demanding certainty? How does the principle 'al-ẓann kāf' challenge your own decision-making?
?
The Companions selected a leader based on what they knew. In your own community, do you trust the collective judgment of believers, or do you insist on perfect conditions before you support a leader? What does your answer say about your faith in the ummah?
?
If you were in a position to select a leader for your community, would you be satisfied with reasonable assumption, or would you demand infallible knowledge? How does this lesson expose your own pride or lack of trust in Allah's guidance through the community?

Objections Three and Four: Fitna and Divine Appointment
Presents third and fourth objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the objections that the election process causes fitna and that the chosen caliph is merely a human appointee, not from God.

Core Idea
Lack of an imam causes greater fitna, and divine law validates the chosen caliph.

The third objection claims that the process of choosing a caliph led to fitna (sedition), as seen in the conflict between Ali and Muawiya. The fourth objection argues that the elected caliph is only a caliph of the people, not of God and His Messenger. The Sunni responses address both: fitna arises only when people ignore truth and preference, and the absence of an imam is worse; the Law confirms that whomever the community chooses is indeed God's caliph.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection and response
Read the Arabic for the third objection: 'أن في ذلك إثارة الفتنة كما في زمن علي ومعاوية' and the response. Notice how the response qualifies the situation.
The response shifts blame to non-submission to truth.
2
Analyze the third response
The response states that the discussion applies when people submit to truth and consider aspects of preponderance. If they don't, fitna occurs. Then it adds that the fitna of lacking an imam is far greater, because the assumption is that there is no text (naṣṣ) for appointment. Otherwise, there is no choice.
This is a key point: without a divinely designated text, the community must choose, and that choice prevents greater fitna.
3
Read the fourth objection and response
Read: 'أن مختار أهل البيعة يكون خليفة منهم لا من الله ورسوله' and the response: 'قام دليل الشرع على أن من اختاروه فهو خليفة الله ورسوله'.
This directly counters the objection by citing the Law's evidence.
4
Reflect on the role of divine law
The fourth response does not provide a specific proof but asserts that the Law itself validates the community's choice. Consider how this argument relies on the authority of the Sharīʿa as a whole.

Source Text
