الأول - أن من الشروط مالا يعلمه أهل البيعة كالعصمة والأفضلية، والعلم بالدين كله .
قلنا لو سلم الاشتراط فالظن كاف.
الثاني - أن ليس إليهم تولية مثل القضاء والاحتساب فهذا أولى.
قلنا : لو سلم فلوجود الإمام.
الثالث - أن في ذلك إثارة الفتنة كما في زمن علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه) ومعاوية .
قلنا: الكلام فيما إذا أذعنوا للحق واعتبروا جهات الترجيح . ولوسلم، ففتنة عدم الإمام أضعاف ذلك، إذ التقدير عدم النص. وإلا فلا اختيار عليه .
الرابع - أن مختار أهل البيعة يكون خليفة منهم لا من الله ورسوله . قلنا : قام دليل الشرع على أن من اختاروه فهو خليفة الله ورسوله .
الخامس - إذا عقد أهلان لأهلين ولم يعلم السبق ، لزم خلو الزمان عن الإمام إذ لا سبيل إلى تصحيحهما ولا إبطالهما ولا تعيين الصحيح منهما، ولا نصب ثالث .
قلنا : بل يرجح أحدهما أو ينصب ثالث، ولا فساد .
السادس - أنه (صلى الله عليه وسلم) لم يكن ترك الاستخلاف في أدنى غيبة، ولا البيان في أدنى ما يحتاج إليه، فكيف في غيبة الوفاة وفي أساس المهمات .
السابع - أن النبي ( أرأف بأمته من الأب لولده، فكيف ترك الوصية لهم إلى أحد ؟
قلنا : التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال .
الثامن - قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم والإمامة من معظمات أمر الدين، فكيف تهمل ؟
قلنا : التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال . )
The engagement of the Companions after the Prophet (peace be upon him) and after Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) in the pledge of allegiance and selection without takbir [؟] and the Shi'a opposed in aspects:
First - that among the conditions are things not known to the people of allegiance, such as infallibility and superiority, and knowledge of the entire religion.
We said: if the condition is granted, then assumption (al-ẓann) is sufficient.
Second - that they are not authorized to appoint positions like judge and market inspector, so this [imamate] is more appropriate.
We said: if granted, then because the imam exists.
Third - that this stirs up sedition (al-fitna) as in the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and Muawiya.
We said: the discussion is when they submit to the truth and consider the aspects of preponderance. And if granted, the sedition of lack of an imam is greater than that, since the assumption is the absence of a text. Otherwise, there is no choice in the matter.
Fourth - that the chosen one of the people of allegiance becomes a caliph from them, not from Allah and His Messenger. We said: the evidence of the Law (al-sharʿ) has established that whomever they choose is the caliph of Allah and His Messenger.
Fifth - if two groups contract [allegiance] for two persons and the precedence is not known, then a period void of an imam becomes necessary, because there is no way to validate both, nor to invalidate both, nor to determine which is correct, nor to appoint a third.
We said: rather, one of them is given preponderance or a third is appointed, and there is no corruption.
Sixth - that he (peace be upon him) did not neglect to appoint a successor in the slightest absence, nor to explain the smallest thing needed, so how [could he neglect] in the absence of death and in the foundation of important matters?
Seventh - that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is more compassionate to his community than a father to his child, so how did he leave the bequest for them to anyone?
We said: delegation to their choice and their independent judgment is a kind of succession, bequest, and completion.
Eighth - His statement, Exalted: Today I have perfected for you your religion, and imamate is among the greatest matters of religion, so how is it neglected? We said: delegation to their choice and their independent judgment is a kind of succession, bequest, and completion.)
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents eight objections raised by the Shi'a against the legitimacy of the caliphate as established by the Companions, and provides Sunni refutations for each. The author lists them in sequence. 
First objection: The people of allegiance do not know all the required conditions for an imam (like infallibility, superiority, full religious knowledge). Response: Even if such conditions are required, a reasonable assumption (ẓann) suffices.
Second: The people of allegiance are not even entrusted with appointing minor officials, so how can they appoint an imam? Response: They can, because appointing an imam is even more important, and the imam exists to authorize them.
Third: The process of election caused sedition, as in the time of Ali and Muawiya. Response: Sedition occurs only when people do not submit to truth and ignore proper considerations. Moreover, the sedition of having no imam is far worse.
Fourth: The elected caliph is chosen by men, not by God. Response: The Law (sharʿ) declares that whomever the community chooses is God's and His Messenger's caliph.
Fifth: If two rival contracts occur, it leads to a period without an imam. Response: One is preferenced or a third is appointed; no corruption follows.
Sixth: The Prophet did not neglect to appoint a successor even for short absences, so why would he for death? Response: (Missing in OCR.)
Seventh: The Prophet is more compassionate than a father, so would he leave no will regarding leadership? Response: Delegating to the community's choice and ijtihad is itself a form of succession and bequest.
Eighth: Verse 'Today I perfected your religion' implies that imamate, being a major matter, would not be neglected. Response: Same as seventh: delegation is perfection.

Key Terms
فتنة
إمام
نص
خليفة
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that Sunni caliphate is a purely human institution without divine approval.
Correction: The response to the fourth objection asserts that divine law itself validates the community's choice, making the caliph's authority divinely sanctioned.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the third objection, what is worse than the fitna of election?
A) The fitna of lacking an imam
B) The fitna of civil war
C) The fitna of hypocrisy
D) The fitna of innovation

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response defends election by arguing that it prevents greater fitna and that divine law confirms the chosen caliph's legitimacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see conflict among Muslims—whether in history or today—do you instinctively look for a single party to blame, or do you search for the truth as this lesson teaches? What prevents you from submitting to truth (idhʿān li-l-ḥaqq) when your own group is involved?
?
The lesson argues that having no imam at all causes greater fitna than any fitna from election. In your own life, do you ever avoid making a difficult decision (choosing an imam, leader, or path) because of fear of conflict, not realizing that indecision itself may cause greater chaos?
?
The fourth response teaches that divine law transforms a community's choice into a divine appointment. Do you truly believe that your collective choices—in family, community, or state—can carry divine weight? How does that change your sense of responsibility in those choices?

4 min
Objection Five: Competing Contracts
Presents fifth objection regarding competing allegiance contracts and the Sunni response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the objection that two competing contracts of allegiance lead to a period without an imam.

Core Idea
Competing claims can be resolved by preference or new appointment.

The fifth objection raises a procedural issue: if two groups each give allegiance to different individuals, and the order (sabq) is unknown, then the world would be without an imam because no contract can be validated or invalidated, and no third can be appointed. The Sunni response simply states that one is given preponderance or a third is appointed, and there is no corruption. This lesson unpacks that brief response.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth objection
Read the Arabic: 'إذا عقد أهلان لأهلين ولم يعلم السبق' — note the scenario of two valid-looking contracts with unknown precedence.
The objection claims impossibility of resolving without imamless period.
2
Understand the dilemma
The objection says: no way to validate both, invalidate both, determine correct one, or appoint a third. So the time between the contracts would have no imam.
3
Read the response
Response: 'بل يرجح أحدهما أو ينصب ثالث، ولا فساد' — rather, one is given preponderance or a third is appointed, and no corruption.
The response is decisive but brief.
4
Reflect on the method of preponderance
The response does not specify how preponderance is determined, but implies that scholars can use methods of tarjīḥ (giving precedence) based on available evidence. Alternatively, a third person can be appointed.
This shows confidence in the community's ability to resolve disputes.

Source Text

الأول - أن من الشروط مالا يعلمه أهل البيعة كالعصمة والأفضلية، والعلم بالدين كله .

Checkpoint
What two solutions does the Sunni response offer to the problem of two competing contracts?
A) Validate both or invalidate both
B) Give preponderance to one or appoint a third

Even in cases of competing claims, the community can resolve via preponderance or new appointment without corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between two loyalties or two 'authorities' in your life, do you feel paralyzed, thinking there is no way out? How does the confident response 'بل يرجح أحدهما أو ينصب ثالث، ولا فساد' challenge your tendency to despair? What specific step can you take today to move toward resolution instead of letting the situation fester?
?
The objector saw only impossible options; the Sunni response shows there is always a valid path forward. Is there a 'deadlock' in your personal life—a relationship, a decision, or a community issue—where you have assumed no good outcome exists? Will you commit to seeking a solution through careful reasoning (tarjih) or even appointing a neutral third party, trusting that corruption is not inevitable? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to break that deadlock.

Objections Six, Seven, Eight: Prophetic Practice and Delegation
Presents sixth (response missing), seventh, and eighth objections with responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni responses to the remaining objections regarding the Prophet's failure to appoint a successor and the perfection of religion, with emphasis on delegation (tafwīḍ) to the community's ijtihad.

Core Idea
The Prophet's delegation to the community's choice is itself a form of succession and perfection.

The final three objections argue from the Prophet's compassion and from the verse on perfection of religion. Objection six (response missing) notes that the Prophet never left even short journeys without appointing a successor, yet he died without explicit succession. Objections seven and eight (with identical responses) point out that the Prophet is more compassionate than a father and that imamate is a major part of religion, so it could not be neglected. The Sunni response is the same for both: delegation (tafwīḍ) to the community's choice and ijtihad is a kind of succession, bequest, and completion.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth objection (response missing)
Read the Arabic for the sixth objection. Note that the response is absent in the text. Consider what the response might be based on the pattern.
The OCR has a gap; we only have the objection.
2
Read the seventh objection and response
Read: 'أن النبي أرأف بأمته من الأب لولده، فكيف ترك الوصية...' The response: 'التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال'.
The response equates delegation with a form of bequest.
3
Read the eighth objection and response
Read the eighth objection citing the verse 'اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم'. The response is identical to the seventh.
This repetition may be an OCR duplication.
4
Understand the concept of tafwīḍ
Tafwīḍ means delegating authority. The response argues that by leaving the choice to the community, the Prophet effectively made a bequest (waṣiyya) and completed the religion. The community's ijtihad is part of the religion's perfection.

According to the Sunni response, what does 'tafwīḍ' (delegation) to the community's choice and ijtihad constitute?
A) Abandonment of religious guidance

D) A rejection of divine law

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you follow the Prophet, but do you trust the community's ijtihad as much as the Prophet trusted it? Or do you demand a clear text for every matter? Which of these two attitudes best describes your heart?
?
The Prophet is more compassionate than a father. Yet you fear that leaving matters to human judgment leads to error. Is your fear a sign of lack of trust in the Prophet's wisdom? What would it take for you to accept the delegation as completion?
?
How often have you used the argument that 'the religion is perfect' to justify not engaging in ijtihad? Have you considered that perfection includes the mechanism of delegation? Are you using the verse to avoid responsibility?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
5 min
The Agreed Principle and the Disputed Rejected Method
Establishes consensus and introduces the first (rejected) disputed method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal consensus that mere qualification does not bestow imamate, and the first disputed method—the Zaydi claim that any Fatimid rising with a sword becomes imam—which is rejected by most scholars.

Core Idea
Imamate is not automatic; it requires a separate contractual act.

Before discussing the variety of methods used to establish an imam, the text establishes a foundational agreement: no one becomes an imam simply by being qualified. There must be an additional act that formally contracts the imamate. The first disputed method the author addresses is a radical Zaydi view that has been almost universally rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview. Notice that the text begins with a consensus point before listing methods.
The passage is long; focus on the first part for this lesson.
2
Identify the agreed principle
Locate the sentence: a man does not become imam merely by suitability and conditions. Understand that this is a universal agreement across sects.
This principle sets up why there must be a distinct 'contract'.
3
Examine the first disputed method
Find the description: a person (originator for imamate) separates from tyrants, enjoins good, forbids evil, and calls to himself. Note that the Zaydi sub-sect (non-Salihiyya) held this view, and only al-Jubba'i agreed.
The text uses the term 'ردّ الدعوة' – the method is rejected.
4
Reflect on why this method is rejected
Ask yourself why most scholars reject this method. Think about the potential for chaos and lack of clear criteria beyond lineage and rebellion.
The commentary explains that this view is limited to a minority.

في طريق ثبوتها اتفقت الأمة على أن الرجل لا يصير إماماً بمجرد صلاحيته للإمامة واجتماع الشرائط فيه بل لا بد من أمر آخر به تنعقد الإمامة وهي طرق، منها متفق عليه ، ومنها مختلف فيه . فالمختلف فيه المردود الدعوة بأن يباين الظلمة من هو أصل للإمامة، ويأمر بالمعروف وينهى عن المنكر، ويدعو إلى اتباعه . قال به غير الصالحية من الزيدية، ذاهبين إلى أن كل فاطمي خرج شاهراً لسيفه، داعياً إلى سبيل ربه فهو إمام . ولم يوافقهم على ذلك إلا الجبائي . والمختلف فيه المقبول عندنا وعند المعتزلة والخوارج ، والصالحية خلافاً للشيعة هو اختيار أهل الحل والعقد وبيعتهم من غير أن يشترط إجماعهم على ذلك، ولا عدد محدود، بل ينعقد بعقد واحد منهم ، ولهذا لم يتوقف أبو بكر (رضي الله تعالى عنه) إلى انتشار الأخبار في الأقطار، ولم ينكر عليه أحد .
وقال عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه لأبي عبيدة : ابسط يدك أبايعك ، فقال : أتقول هذا وأبو بكر حاضر ، فبايع أبا بكر ، وهذا مذهب الأشعري . إلا أنه يشترط أن يكون العقد بمشهد من الشهود لئلا يدعي آخر أنه عقد عقداً سراً متقدماً على هذا العقد. وذهب أكثر المعتزلة إلى اشتراط عدد خمسة ممن يصلح للإمامة أخذاً من
Regarding the method of its establishment, the community (al-umma) has agreed that a man does not become an imam merely by his suitability for imamate and the gathering of conditions in him, but there must be another thing by which the imamate is contracted. And it has methods, some agreed upon and some disputed. The disputed and rejected method is that one who is a root [؟] for imamate should separate from the tyrants, enjoin good, forbid evil, and call people to follow him. The non-Salihiyya among the Zaydiyya held this, going to the extent that every Fatimid who goes out unsheathing his sword, calling to the way of his Lord, is an imam. Only al-Jubba'i agreed with them on that. The disputed but accepted method among us [Ash'aris], the Mu'tazila, the Khawarij, and the Salihiyya, contrary to the Shi'a, is the choice of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd) and their pledge of allegiance, without requiring their consensus nor a fixed number; rather, it is contracted by a single contract from them. For this reason, Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) did not wait for news to spread in the regions, and no one objected to him. And Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said to Abu Ubayda: 'Stretch out your hand that I may pledge allegiance to you,' and he said: 'Do you say this while Abu Bakr is present?' So he pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr. This is the school of al-Ash'ari, except that he stipulates that the contract be in the presence of witnesses, lest another claim that he made a secret contract preceding this contract. And most of the Mu'tazila went to requiring the number of five of those fit for imamate, taking from
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage discusses the procedural methods for establishing the imamate. It begins by stating a consensus: a man does not become imam merely by possessing the qualifications; there must be an additional act that constitutes the contract of imamate. This contract has various methods, some agreed upon (by all) and some disputed.

The author then describes the first disputed method, which is rejected: that a person who has a root connection to imamate (apparently meaning a Fatimid, as later clarified) should separate himself from the tyrants, command good, forbid evil, and call people to follow him. This view is held by the non-Salihiyya among the Zaydiyya, who go so far as to say that any Fatimid who rises with his sword unsheathed calling to the way of his Lord is an imam. Only al-Jubba'i agreed with them on this.

Then the author presents the second disputed method, which is accepted by the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Salihiyya (as opposed to the Shi'a): it is the choice of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʻaqd) and their pledge of allegiance. They do not require their consensus, nor a fixed number; rather, the imamate is contracted by a single contract from among them. The author supports this by noting that Abu Bakr did not wait for news to spread across the regions before being accepted, and no one objected. He also cites the event where Umar proposed to pledge allegiance to Abu Ubayda, who refused due to Abu Bakr being present, and then pledged to Abu Bakr.

This is the school of al-Ash'ari, except that he stipulates that the contract must be in the presence of witnesses to prevent someone from falsely claiming a prior secret contract.

Finally, the author notes that most Mu'tazila require a number of five people fit for imamate, basing this on analogy.

Key Terms
طريق ثبوتها
بيعة
متفق عليه
مختلف فيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply being qualified (e.g., learned, just, from Quraysh) automatically makes one the imam.
Correction: The text clarifies that qualifications are necessary but insufficient; a formal contract (by election or designation) is required.

Checkpoint
What is the unanimous agreement mentioned in the passage?
A) The imam must be a Fatimid.
B) A man does not become imam merely by his suitability and conditions.
C) The imam is chosen by ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd.
D) The imamate is established by a pledge of allegiance from five people.

Key Takeaway
The first requirement for establishing imamate is a formal contract; the Zaydi method of armed uprising is rejected by the majority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at Muslim leaders today, do you instinctively respect them because of their titles or qualifications, or do you require a clear 'contract' — a formal pledge of allegiance from the community? In what way might your own attitude reflect the Zaydi error of equating merit with authority?
?
The Zaydi method demands that an imam physically rise against tyrants. Have you ever been in a situation where you remained silent about injustice even though you felt qualified to speak? What held you back — and does that silence make you complicit in the tyranny?
?
Only al-Jubba'i supported this method. Imagine standing with an opinion that almost all other scholars reject. How do you react when your deeply held conviction is dismissed by the majority? Does your confidence come from evidence or from pride?

The Accepted Disputed Method: Election by the People of Loosening and Binding
Details the accepted method of election by ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of Islamic sects (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Salihiyya) accept that the imamate is contracted by the choice and pledge of the people of loosening and binding, without requiring consensus or a fixed number, and that al-Ash'ari added the condition of witnesses.

Core Idea
The imam is chosen by the community's notables, not by divine appointment.

After dismissing the Zaydi method, the text turns to the second disputed method—the one that is accepted by most sects. This method centers on the role of ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd, the qualified representatives of the community who have the authority to elect and pledge allegiance to an imam. The passage also provides historical evidence from the caliphate of Abu Bakr and details the conditions added by al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the lines beginning with 'والمختلف فيه المقبول'. Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
This part is independent from the previous lesson.
2
Identify the accepted method and its proponents
Note that the method is 'choice of ahl al-ḥall wa al-ʿaqd and their pledge'. It is accepted by Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Khawarij, and Salihiyya, but rejected by Shi'a.
3
Understand the historical example
The text cites Abu Bakr's caliphate: he did not wait for news to spread, and no one objected. Also mentions Umar's offer to Abu Ubayda, who deferred to Abu Bakr.
This shows that a single pledge can suffice.
4
Learn al-Ash'ari's condition
Al-Ash'ari requires the contract to be in the presence of witnesses to prevent secret claims. Most Mu'tazila require five qualified electors.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; the text is incomplete.

Seventh and Eighth Proofs for Imamate and the Answer
Objection and response: two proofs for imamate and the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs based on the Prophet's compassion and the perfection of religion, and the author's response that delegation to ijtihad is itself a kind of appointment.

Core Idea
Delegation to community choice is itself a kind of prophetic appointment.

In this passage, the author responds to two common arguments for the necessity of explicit appointment of an imam. The first appeals to the Prophet's compassion, the second to the Quranic verse on perfection of religion. The author argues that delegating the choice to the community does not contradict either point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two proofs followed by the author's response.
The opening phrase 'يحتاج إليه' connects from the previous page.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the seventh proof (Prophet's compassion) and the eighth proof (perfection of religion). Understand how each argues for an explicit appointment.
Both are based on what the Prophet would or must have done.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author says 'delegating to their choice and ijtihad is a kind of appointment, recommendation, and completion.' Reflect on how this answers both proofs.
The response is compact: it covers both proofs simultaneously.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Consider how this refutes the necessity of explicit text (nass) for imamate. The community's ijtihad is sufficient.
This is a key Sunni response to Shia arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحتاج إليه، فكيف في غيبة الوفاة وفي أساس المهمات . السابع - أن النبي ( أرأف بأمته من الأب لولده، فكيف ترك الوصية لهم إلى أحد ؟ الثامن - قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم (۱) والإمامة من معظمات أمر الدين، فكيف تهمل ؟ قلنا : التفويض إلى اختيارهم واجتهادهم نوع استخلاف وتوصية وإكمال . )
Needs it. So how about in the absence of death and in the foundation of important matters? Seventh: That the Prophet (ﷺ) is more compassionate to his community than a father to his child, so how did he leave a will (wasiyyah) for them to someone? Eighth: His saying, exalted: 'Today I have perfected your religion for you' (5:3), and the imamate is among the greatest matters of the religion, so how is it neglected? We said: Delegating to their choice and ijtihad is a kind of appointment, recommendation, and completion.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response pair. The objector raises two proofs: (1) the Prophet's compassion would have compelled him to explicitly appoint a successor by will; (2) the verse about perfection of religion implies that the imamate, being a major matter, must have been explicitly addressed. The author responds that delegating the choice to the community's ijtihad is itself a form of appointment and fulfills the perfection of religion. This refutes the claim that the Prophet neglected his duty.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the perfection of religion requires explicit textual appointment for every detail, so imamate must have been explicitly designated.
Correction: The author argues that delegation to communal ijtihad is itself a form of appointment and completion, so the verse does not require explicit text for imamate.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet's compassion is described as greater than a father's. When you delegate important decisions to others, do you do so out of trust and care, or out of neglect? How can you imitate the Prophet's compassion in the way you entrust responsibilities?
?
The verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' is often understood to mean that every detail is spelled out. But the response says perfection includes delegation to ijtihad. Are you willing to accept uncertainty and communal reasoning in matters where no explicit text exists, or do you demand a clear proof for everything? What would change in your faith if you embraced delegation as part of perfection?
?
You believe the religion is complete. But if imamate—a central political matter—is left to the community's choice, what does that say about your own role in shaping religious leadership? Are you actively participating in the ijtihad of your community, or relying on others to decide for you?

Establishing Imamate: Choice and Bay'ah
Classification of ways to establish imamate and proof for bay'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on how imamate is established, the two disputed methods, and the proof from the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Imamate is established by choice and bay'ah, not by qualification alone or armed revolt.

In this passage, the author clarifies that mere qualification does not make one imam; an additional act is needed. He examines two disputed methods—one rejected (the Zaydi view of rising with the sword) and one accepted (bay'ah by the people of binding and loosing)—and then proves the validity of choice using the consensus of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the English translation. Notice the author's structure: first a general agreement, then a classification of ways.
2
Identify the two disputed ways
Find the two types of 'mukhtalaf fih': one rejected (Zaydi view) and one accepted (bay'ah view). Understand why each is classified as such.
The accepted view is that of Sunnis, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij but not Shi'a.
3
Examine the examples and conditions
Look at the examples of Abu Bakr and Umar, and note the Ash'ari condition of witnesses and the Mu'tazili condition of five. See how the author uses these to illustrate flexibility.
The single bay'ah suffices, as shown by Abu Bakr's swift acceptance.
4
Analyze the proof from consensus
The author argues that the way must be either text (nass) or choice. Since text is absent for Abu Bakr and Ali, and the Companions consistently used choice without objection, this constitutes consensus that choice is valid.
This refutes the Shi'a position requiring nass.

وقال عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه لأبي عبيدة : ابسط يدك أبايعك ، فقال : أتقول هذا وأبو بكر حاضر ، فبايع أبا بكر ، وهذا مذهب الأشعري (٤). إلا أنه يشترط أن يكون العقد بمشهد من الشهود لئلا يدعي آخر أنه عقد عقداً سراً متقدماً على هذا العقد. وذهب أكثر المعتزلة إلى اشتراط عدد خمسة ممن يصلح للإمامة أخذاً من أمر الشورى لنا على كون البيعة والاختيار طريقاً أن الطريق إما النص وإما الاختيار. والنص منتف في حق أبي بكر رضي الله تعالى عنه مع كونه إماماً بالإجماع ، وكذا في حق علي عند التحقيق. وأيضاً اشتغل الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم بعد وفاة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم ) (۱) ومقتل عثمان (رضي الله تعالى عنه) باختيار الإمام، وعقد البيعة من غير نكير، فكان إجماعاً على كونه طريقاً ، ولا عبرة بمخالفة الشيعة بعد ذلك
Regarding the way of its establishment, the community agreed that a man does not become imam merely by his qualification for the imamate and the gathering of conditions in him, but there must be another matter by which the imamate is contracted. These are ways: some agreed upon, some disputed. As for the disputed one which is rejected: that one who is superior to the oppressor comes out for the imamate, commands good, forbids evil, and calls people to follow him. This is held by non-Salihi Zaydis, going to the view that every Fatimid who comes out brandishing his sword calling to the way of his Lord is an imam. Only al-Jubba'i agreed with them on that. And the disputed one which is accepted among us, the Mu'tazila, and the Khawarij, and the Salihiyya (3) contrary to the Shi'a, is the choice of the people of binding and loosing and their bay'ah, without requiring their consensus on that, nor a limited number. Rather, it is contracted by the contract of a single one of them. Therefore, Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) did not wait for news to spread to the regions, and no one objected to him. And Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said to Abu Ubayda: 'Extend your hand so I may give you bay'ah.' He said: 'Do you say this while Abu Bakr is present?' So he gave bay'ah to Abu Bakr. This is the madhhab of al-Ash'ari (4). However, he stipulates that the contract be in the presence of witnesses so that no one else claims that he made a secret contract prior to this contract. Most of the Mu'tazila went to stipulating a number of five from those qualified for the imamate, taking from the matter of the shura. For us, the fact that bay'ah and choice are a way is based on the fact that the way is either text or choice. Text is absent in the case of Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) despite his being imam by consensus, and likewise in the case of Ali upon investigation. Also, the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) after the death of the Prophet (ﷺ) and the killing of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) occupied themselves with choosing the imam and executing the bay'ah without any objection, so it was a consensus (ijma') that it is a way. No heed is paid to the opposition of the Shi'a after that.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a proof. The author first notes that the umma agrees that qualification alone does not make one imam; an additional act is needed, and there are multiple ways, some agreed upon (muttafaq 'alayh) and some disputed (mukhtalaf fih). He then explains two types of disputed ways: one is the 'rejected' view of the Zaydis that any Fatimid who rises with the sword becomes imam; the other is the 'accepted' view of Sunnis, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij (but not Shi'a) that imamate is established by the choice (ikhtiyar) of the people of binding and loosing (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) through bay'ah. He gives examples: the swift bay'ah of Abu Bakr without waiting for distant regions, and Umar's offer to Abu Ubayda. He then mentions the Ash'ari condition of witnesses and the Mu'tazili requirement of five. The proof is then presented: the method of establishing imamate is either by explicit text (nass) or by choice. Since nass is absent for Abu Bakr (and also for Ali upon examination), and since the Companions after the Prophet's death and after Uthman's death engaged in choice and bay'ah without objection, there is consensus (ijma') that choice is valid. Therefore, any opposition from the Shi'a is disregarded.

Key Terms
أهل الحل والعقد
البيعة
الاختيار
النص
الإجماع
الشورى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imamate can only be established by explicit divine text (nass).
Correction: The author proves that choice (ikhtiyar) via bay'ah is valid, as demonstrated by the unanimous practice of the Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni position in this passage, how is an imam established?
A) Only by explicit appointment from the previous imam.
B) By bay'ah of one qualified person from the people of binding and loosing.
C) By election of the entire community.
D) By divine designation.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is that imamate is validly established through bay'ah by the people of binding and loosing, supported by the consensus of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader chosen by the community you trust, do you accept their authority wholeheartedly, or do you internally demand a 'text'—some perfect qualification—before you follow? What does your hesitation reveal about your trust in collective wisdom?
?
The Sahaba acted decisively in giving bay'ah to Abu Bakr without waiting for every region's approval. Where in your life are you delaying obedience to Allah or His Messenger because you are waiting for more confirmation or a 'better' leader?
?
If you were among the people of binding and loosing today, would you have the courage to step forward and choose a leader, even if imperfect, for the sake of unity? Or would you retreat, leaving the decision to others and then criticize later?

First Shi'a Objection: Infallibility and Knowledge
The author records the first Shi'a objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Shi'a objection regarding the imam's infallibility and knowledge, and how it is refuted by denying its premises.

Core Idea
Infallibility is not required for imamate; probable knowledge suffices.

The first Shi'a objection asserts that the imam must be infallible and possess complete religious knowledge, and that these qualities cannot be known through election. This claim is central to the Shi'a conception of imamate. The author refutes it by challenging both premises, arguing that infallibility is not a necessary condition and that the electors can form probable judgments about the imam's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the response introduced by 'ورد'. Focus on understanding the two claims and the two denials.
The response is very concise: it denies both premises.
2
Identify the two premises of the objection
The objection has two main premises: (1) The imam must be infallible, superior, and all-knowing. (2) There is no way to know these by election. Identify these in the text.
The second premise is about the impossibility of knowing through election.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation denies both premises. First, the conditions are not required for imamate (as argued earlier). Second, the electors (people of binding and loosing) can acquire probable knowledge (zann) of these attributes, so it is possible to know them by election.
The key point: 'zann' (probable knowledge) is sufficient, not certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - أن الإمام يجب أن يكون معصوماً أفضل من رعيته . عالماً بأمر الدين كله ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك بالاختيار، ورد بمنع المقدمتين فقد سبق عدم اشتراط الأمور، وعلم بالضرورة حصول الظن لأهل الحل والعقد بالصفات المذكورة.
First: That the imam must be infallible (ma'sum), superior to his subjects, knowledgeable of all religious affairs, and there is no way to know that by election. Answered by denying both premises. Indeed, the non-requirement of these conditions has preceded, and it is known necessarily that the people of binding and loosing acquire probable knowledge (zann) of the mentioned attributes.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its response. The Shi'a objection states that the imam must be infallible (ma'sum), superior to his subjects, and all-knowing in religion, and claims that these qualities cannot be known through human election. The response denies both premises: first, that these conditions are not required for imamate (as argued earlier); second, that it is not impossible to know them by election, because the people of binding and loosing can obtain probable knowledge (zann) of these attributes through their assessment. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
معصوم
ظن
اختيار
أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infallibility (isma) is a necessary condition for imamate and that it cannot be known through human election.
Correction: The author argues that infallibility is not a required condition, and even if it were, probable knowledge (zann) through election is sufficient.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that the imam must be infallible and that this cannot be known by election?
A) He agrees but says it can be known through miracles.
B) He denies that infallibility is required and says probable knowledge suffices.
C) He says election can produce certainty.
D) He says the imam does not need to be known.

Key Takeaway
The first Shi'a objection is refuted by denying that infallibility is required for imamate and that it cannot be known by election, since probable knowledge (zann) from the electors is sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own evaluation of religious leaders, do you often demand absolute certainty about their qualities, or are you willing to trust probable knowledge (zann) as sufficient? How might this lesson challenge your expectations?
?
Have you ever hesitated to accept a leader or scholar because you felt they were not 'perfect' enough? What does this refutation of infallibility as a requirement say about your own standards?
?
When you participate in communal decisions—whether in the mosque, family, or workplace—do you strive to exercise the responsibility of 'ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd' by seeking probable knowledge before choosing? How can you improve?

Second Shi'a Objection: Inability of Bay'ah to Confer Authority
The author records the second Shi'a objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument that bay'ah cannot confer supreme authority and the Sunni response based on the permissibility of arbitration (tahkim).

Core Idea
Delegation of authority does not require prior direct power over individuals.

The second Shi'a objection claims that the people of bay'ah (electors) are incapable of appointing even a judge, so how can they appoint the imam? In this passage, the author refutes this by denying the minor premise and invoking the concept of arbitration. He also clarifies that in the absence of an imam, the electors become the source of investiture.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Shi'a argument: the electors cannot even appoint a judge or market inspector, so they cannot appoint the supreme leader.
Notice the logical structure: they argue from a lesser incapacity to a greater one.
2
Analyze the refutation
The author denies the minor premise and uses the analogy of arbitration (tahkim) to show that delegation is possible.
He also adds a conditional: if it were granted, it's only when an imam exists.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation upholds the validity of bay'ah as a method of appointing the imam in Sunni thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن أهل البيعة لا يقدرون على تولية مثل القضاء والاحتساب، ولا على التصرف في فرد من آحاد الأمة، فكيف يقدرون على تولية الرياسة الكبرى وعلى أقدار الغير على التصرف في أمر الدين والدنيا لكافة الأمة . ورد بمنع الصغرى، فإن التحكيم جائز عندنا. والشاهد يجعل القاضي قادراً على التصرف في الغير، ولو سلم فذلك لوجود من إليه التولية وهو الإمام، ولا كذلك إذا مات ولا إمام غيره .
Second: That the people of bay'ah are incapable of appointing someone to positions like judgeship and hisbah, and cannot act upon any individual of the community. So how can they appoint the great leadership and empower others to act in religious and worldly affairs for the entire community? Answered by denying the minor premise. Indeed, arbitration (tahkim) is permissible in our view. And the witness makes the judge able to act upon others. And even if it were granted, that is due to the existence of the one to whom the appointment belongs, i.e., the imam. Not so when he dies and there is no other imam.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response. The Shi'a objection argues that the people who give bay'ah (the electors) themselves lack the authority to appoint even a judge or market inspector, so how can they appoint the supreme leader and delegate authority over the entire community? The response denies the minor premise (that they are incapable). The author uses the analogy of arbitration (tahkim): a witness can empower a judge to rule over others. Similarly, the electors can delegate authority. Moreover, he notes that normally such delegation occurs in the presence of an imam, but when there is no imam, the electors act as the source of investiture.

Key Terms
البيعة
التحكيم
القضاء
الاحتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the electors must have direct authority over individuals to be able to appoint an imam.
Correction: The author shows that delegation through arbitration is valid, so they can confer authority without direct control.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the claim that the people of bay'ah cannot confer authority?
A) The appointment of judges.
B) The prohibition of interest.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The contract of marriage.

Key Takeaway
The delegation of authority through bay'ah is valid because arbitration (tahkim) demonstrates that one can empower another without prior direct authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt powerless to make a decision because you lacked formal authority? How does the example of the witness empowering the judge in tahkim challenge your assumption that authority must come from above? This week, will you delegate a responsibility you think you cannot delegate?
?
The Shi'a objection assumes that only the imam can appoint. When a leader in your life—at home, work, or in the community—is absent, do you step up to take responsibility or wait for someone else? What is stopping you from acting as the electors did?
?
The refutation shows that ordinary people can be the source of legitimate authority. In what areas of your life do you avoid exercising the authority you actually have? What would change if you embraced the truth that you can empower others even without prior power?

Third Shi'a Objection: Bay'ah and Fitna
Third Shi'a objection and its refutation regarding bay'ah causing fitna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Shi'a objection that the bay'ah process itself causes fitna, and how the author refutes it by reinterpreting the conflict between Ali and Muawiya as a procedural dispute, not a rejection of imamate.

Core Idea
Disagreement over timing is not the same as rejection of authority.

This lesson examines the third Shi'a objection that the bay'ah process itself causes fitna, defeating the purpose of the imamate. The author refutes this by arguing that fitna only arises when people fail to submit to the truth, and that the historical conflict between Ali and Muawiya was not about the legitimacy of Ali's imamate but about a procedural issue. We will follow the objection and its answer step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection states that bay'ah leads to fitna, using the example of Ali and Muawiya.
The objection claims that the very process of bay'ah contradicts the purpose of imamate.
2
Identify the refutation's first part
The author first responds that fitna does not occur when people submit to the truth. Understand that the problem is not bay'ah itself but lack of submission.
This part argues that the criteria for preference (e.g., precedence) are known from Sharia, so proper bay'ah should not cause fitna.
3
Analyze the historical reinterpretation
The author reinterprets the conflict between Ali and Muawiya as being about the timing of bay'ah (before qisas), not about the validity of Ali's imamate. This shows that the objection's example does not support its claim.
This distinction is key: procedural disputes are not challenges to the imamate itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإمامة لإزالة الفتن وإثباتها بالبيعة مظنة إثارة الفتن لاختلاف الآراء، كما في زمن علي رضي الله تعالى عنه ومعاوية، فتعود على موضوعها بالنقض . ورد بأنه لا فتنة عند الانقياد للحق . فإن جهات الترجيح من السبق وغير معلومة من الشريعة . ونزاع معاوية لم يكن في إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه) بل في أنه هل يجب عليه بيعته قبل الاقتصاص من قتلة عثمان؟ وأما عند الترفع والاستيلاء
Third: That the imamate is meant to remove civil strife (fitan), but establishing it by bay'ah is a likely cause of strife due to differences of opinion, as in the time of Ali and Muawiya, so it would negate its own purpose. Answered: There is no strife when submitting to the truth. For the aspects of preference (such as precedence etc.) are not known from the Sharia. And the conflict with Muawiya was not about the imamate of Ali, but rather about whether he should pledge allegiance before taking retribution from the murderers of Uthman. And as for when it comes to elevation and seizure...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its response. The Shi'a objection claims that the very purpose of imamate is to remove fitna (civil strife), but the process of bay'ah often leads to fitna, as seen in the conflict between Ali and Muawiya, thus defeating its own purpose. The response refutes this by arguing that fitna only arises when people fail to submit to the truth (al-haqq); if they follow the correct criteria, there is no fitna. The author adds that the conflict between Ali and Muawiya was not about the legitimacy of Ali's imamate, but about whether Muawiya should give bay'ah before the qisas (retribution) for Uthman's blood. This redefines the historical event, showing it was a procedural dispute, not a rejection of Ali's imamate. The response continues with 'And as for when it comes to elevation and seizure...' but is incomplete here.

Key Terms
الفتن
البيعة
الترجيح
السبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any disagreement over the imamate leads to fitna, and that the conflict between Ali and Muawiya was a rejection of Ali's imamate.
Correction: The author distinguishes between conflict over the imamate itself and procedural disputes, showing that the latter does not negate the legitimacy of the imamate or the bay'ah system.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what was the actual issue in the conflict between Ali and Muawiya?
A) The legitimacy of Ali's imamate.
B) Whether Muawiya should pledge allegiance before qisas.
C) The succession of Uthman.
D) The authority of the Caliph.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because submitting to the truth prevents fitna, and historical conflicts like that of Ali and Muawiya are often procedural, not fundamental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone who has rightful authority over you—whether a parent, teacher, or leader—do you honestly examine whether your disagreement is about a core principle or merely about timing and procedure? Could a procedural delay be masking a deeper rebellion in your heart?
?
The author states, 'There is no strife when submitting to the truth.' In what area of your life have you been resisting submission to a clear truth—perhaps about your own sins, relationships, or duties—because it would require immediate change? Will you submit today, or will you let fitna grow?
?
Think of a conflict you have been part of. Did you ever claim a procedural objection (like 'it's not the right time' or 'the method is wrong') while actually rejecting the person's authority or the truth itself? What would it take for you to lay down that objection and submit wholeheartedly?

The Sunni Principle of Imamate by Election
Establishes the legitimacy of bay'ah and ikhtiyar for imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni scholars hold that the imam can be elected through bay'ah and choice, based on the historical practice of the Companions and the absence of explicit textual designation for the early caliphs.

Core Idea
The election of the imam is validated by the consensus of the Companions, not by a specific text.

In this passage, the author presents the foundational Sunni argument for the legitimacy of electing the imam. He contrasts the Sunni view with the Shia insistence on textual designation (nass), showing that the early Muslim community consistently used choice (ikhtiyar) and allegiance (bay'ah) to appoint leaders. This practice became a consensus (ijma'), which Sunnis regard as a valid source of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own concise argument come through.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two paths to imamate
The author states that the method of appointing an imam is either textual designation (nass) or choice (ikhtiyar). Understand what each means.
These are the only two possibilities discussed.
3
Examine the evidence against nass
The author argues that nass is not proven for Abu Bakr or Ali, yet both were imams. This implies that ikhtiyar was used.
4
Consider the Companions' practice
After the Prophet's death and after 'Uthman's murder, the Companions chose the imam and gave bay'ah with no disapproval, forming a tacit consensus.
This consensus is the key proof for ikhtiyar.
5
Understand the dismissal of Shia objections
The author concludes that Shia objections after the consensus are irrelevant. The passage then transitions to list the Shia's arguments.
The lesson focuses on the Sunni position only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمر الشورى لنا على كون البيعة والاختيار طريقاً أن الطريق إما النص وإما الاختيار. والنص منتف في حق أبي بكر رضي الله تعالى عنه مع كونه إماماً بالإجماع ، وكذا في حق علي عند التحقيق. وأيضاً اشتغل الصحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم بعد وفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ومقتل عثمان رضي الله تعالى عنه باختيار الإمام، وعقد البيعة من غير نكير، فكان إجماعاً على كونه طريقاً ، ولا عبرة بمخالفة الشيعة بعد ذلك احتجت الشيعة بوجوه :
The matter of consultation (shura) for us is that the path of allegiance (bay'ah) and choice (ikhtiyar) is a way, indeed the way is either textual designation (nass) or choice (ikhtiyar). And nass is absent in the case of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, despite his being imam by consensus, and likewise in the case of Ali upon investigation. Also, the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and the murder of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, occupied themselves with choosing the imam and making the pledge of allegiance without any objection, so it was a consensus (ijma') on it being a way, and no attention is paid to the opposition of the Shia after that. The Shia argued with arguments:
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement establishing the Sunni position that the method of appointing the imam is either textual designation (nass) from the Prophet or choice (ikhtiyar) by the community. The author argues that nass is not established for Abu Bakr or Ali by definitive proof, yet both were accepted as imams—Abu Bakr by consensus and Ali by investigation, implying choice. He then cites the historical practice of the Companions after the Prophet's death and after Uthman's murder, who elected imams without disapproval, thereby forming a tacit consensus (ijma') that election is a valid method. The argument concludes that the Shia objection is irrelevant because their dissent occurred after this consensus. The passage then transitions to list the Shia's arguments.

Key Terms
الشورى
البيعة
الاختيار
النص
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Sunni position denies any possibility of textual designation (nass) for the imam.
Correction: The passage does not claim that nass is impossible, only that it is not proven for the early caliphs, so election is a valid alternative.

Checkpoint
What is the Sunni argument for the validity of choice (ikhtiyar) in appointing the imam?
A) There is explicit textual designation for Abu Bakr
B) The Companions practiced it after the Prophet's death without objection, forming consensus
C) The Shia agreed to it
D) It is the only method mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on imamate is that election through bay'ah and choice is valid, based on the consensus of the Companions and the absence of explicit textual designation for the early caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the process of choosing leaders in your own community (family, masjid, etc.), do you rely more on personal preference or on collective consultation (shura)? How does the Companions' example of bay'ah challenge your approach?
?
The passage argues that the absence of a clear text (nass) does not invalidate legitimate leadership. Is there an area in your life where you demand explicit instructions from Allah before acting, when instead you could rely on the consensus and wisdom of the community?
?
The Companions acted without objection, forming a consensus. Are there times when you have remained silent or objected to a communal decision? How does this lesson call you to examine your own role in building unity among Muslims?

Refuting the Claim of Infallibility as a Condition for Imamah
Refutation of Shia objection on infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shia objection that the imam must be infallible and superior by denying both its premises.

Core Idea
Infallibility is not a necessary condition for the imamate.

The Shia argue that the imam must be infallible and the best, and that this cannot be known through election. This short passage shows how the Sunni scholar refutes that by denying both parts of the argument: first, that such qualities are required, and second, that they cannot be known. The refutation relies on earlier discussions and the concept of probable knowledge (zann) of the electors.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the passage carefully. It states the Shia claim: the imam must be infallible, superior, and knowledgeable, and these cannot be known by choice.
Notice that the objection assumes these qualities are essential and unknowable through human selection.
2
Identify the two premises denied
The refutation says 'by denying both premises'. The first premise is the requirement of infallibility etc. The second premise is that these cannot be known by election.
The author references earlier proof that these matters are not required.
3
Understand the second denial
The refutation states that the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) can obtain probable knowledge of the candidate's attributes. This is how the second premise is denied.
4
Connect to the lesson's core idea
Reflect on why this refutation is important: it defends the Sunni position that the imam does not need to be infallible, and that election by qualified scholars is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - أن الإمام يجب أن يكون معصوماً أفضل من رعيته . عالماً بأمر الدين كله ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك بالاختيار، ورد بمنع المقدمتين فقد سبق عدم اشتراط الأمور، وعلم بالضرورة حصول الظن لأهل الحل والعقد بالصفات المذكورة.
First: that the imam must be infallible (ma'sum), superior to his subjects, knowledgeable of the entire matter of religion, and there is no way to know that by choice (ikhtiyar). It is refuted by denying both premises: indeed, the non-requirement of these matters has preceded, and it is known necessarily that the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) obtain probable knowledge (zann) of the mentioned attributes.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the first Shia objection and its refutation. The objection argues that the imam must be infallible (ma'sum), superior to the community, and fully knowledgeable of religion, and that such qualities cannot be ascertained through the process of election (ikhtiyar). The refutation denies both premises: first, the authors have already proven (in earlier discussion) that these qualities are not conditions for the imamate; second, even if they were, the electors (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) can attain probable knowledge (zann) about a candidate's possession of these attributes through their expertise and observation. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
معصوما
أهل الحل والعقد
الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imam must be infallible and the best, and these cannot be known through election.
Correction: Infallibility is not a condition for the imamate, and the electors can gain probable knowledge of a candidate's attributes.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the first Shia objection that the imam must be known to be infallible through election?
A) He agrees but says infallibility is known through miracles
B) He denies both premises: infallibility is not required, and the electors can obtain probable knowledge of attributes
C) He says the imam does not need to be knowledgeable
D) He says the Prophet explicitly designated the imam

Key Takeaway
The Shia claim that the imam must be infallible and superior is refuted by denying both that these qualities are required and that they cannot be known by election.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose leaders in your own community, do you secretly demand perfection (infallibility) that no human can meet? How does the lesson's emphasis on probable knowledge (zann) challenge your expectation of absolute certainty in important decisions? Are you willing to trust God's guidance through the collective expertise of trustworthy people, even when it feels uncertain?
?
The Shia claim that the imam must be superior to all—do you harbor a secret expectation that your leaders should never make mistakes? How does that expectation keep you from submitting to the wisdom of ahl al-hall wa al-aqd? What would it look like for you to humbly accept leaders who are fallible yet qualified?
?
This refutation reminds us that perfect knowledge of a leader's character is not required; what matters is the best available knowledge (zann). Reflect on a time when you refused to follow someone because you lacked absolute certainty about them. How does this lesson call you to trust God's arrangement for leadership, even when it feels risky?

Refuting the Claim that Electors Cannot Appoint the Imam
Refutes the claim that the electors cannot empower the imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shia objection that the electors are incapable of appointing the imam, using the principle of arbitration and analogy with a witness.

Core Idea
Even ordinary people can delegate authority through arbitration, as when a witness empowers a judge.

This passage tackles a key Shia objection: if the people who elect the imam cannot even appoint a minor official, how can they appoint the supreme leader? The refutation shows that in Sunni jurisprudence, ordinary people can delegate authority through arbitration, drawing an analogy with a witness who empowers a judge. This lesson unpacks the logic of the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Shia objection carefully: it claims that the electors cannot appoint even a judge or muhtasib, so how can they appoint the imam? Notice the logical structure of the argument.
2
Identify the minor premise
The objection's minor premise is that electors cannot appoint anyone. The refutation denies this minor premise.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that arbitration (tahkim) is permissible, and gives the analogy of a witness (shahid) who makes a judge capable of disposal. This shows that ordinary people can delegate authority.
4
Consider the concession
Even if one grants the incapacity normally, the situation is different when the imam dies and there is no imam to appoint; necessity changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن أهل البيعة لا يقدرون على تولية مثل القضاء والاحتساب، ولا على التصرف في فرد من آحاد الأمة، فكيف يقدرون على تولية الرياسة الكبرى وعلى أقدار الغير على التصرف في أمر الدين والدنيا لكافة الأمة . ورد بمنع الصغرى، فإن التحكيم جائز عندنا. والشاهد يجعل القاضي قادراً على التصرف في الغير، ولو سلم فذلك لوجود من إليه التولية وهو الإمام، ولا كذلك إذا مات ولا إمام غيره .
Second: that the people of allegiance (ahl al-bay'ah) are not capable of appointing someone like a judge or a muhtasib, nor of disposing (tasarruf) over any individual of the community, so how can they be capable of appointing the supreme leadership (al-ri'asa al-kubra) and of empowering others to dispose in matters of religion and this world for the entire community? It is refuted by denying the minor premise: indeed, arbitration (tahkim) is permissible with us, and the witness (shahid) makes the judge capable of disposing over others, and if granted, that is due to the existence of the one to whom the appointment belongs, which is the imam, and not so when he dies and there is no imam other than him.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shia objection and its refutation. The objection argues that the people who give allegiance (ahl al-bay'ah) are not capable of appointing even minor officials like judges or market inspectors (muhtasib), so how can they be capable of appointing the supreme imam who has authority over all? The refutation denies the minor premise: the author asserts that arbitration (tahkim) is permissible in Sunni jurisprudence, meaning that ordinary people can delegate authority. Citing analogy with a witness (shahid) who, by testimony, empowers a judge to act, the electors similarly empower the imam. Moreover, even if one grants the incapacity in normal circumstances, that is because there is already an imam to appoint; when the imam dies and there is no successor, the community acts out of necessity to appoint one. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
البيعة
التولية
التحكيم
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The electors cannot appoint anyone, so they cannot appoint the imam.
Correction: In Sunni jurisprudence, ordinary people can delegate authority through arbitration (tahkim), analogously to how a witness empowers a judge.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the claim that the electors cannot appoint the imam?
A) The analogy of a father appointing a guardian
B) The analogy of a witness (shahid) empowering a judge
C) The analogy of a king appointing a vizier
D) The analogy of a slave being freed

Key Takeaway
The people can delegate authority through arbitration, just as a witness empowers a judge, so electors can appoint the imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the leaders you follow in your community, do you see yourself as a passive subject or as a witness who empowers them through your bay'ah? How does this lesson challenge your sense of responsibility in choosing and supporting leaders?
?
This lesson uses the analogy of a witness empowering a judge. In your daily interactions, do you realize the power you have to delegate authority to others? Are you careful about whom you empower, or do you give your bay'ah carelessly?
?
The refutation emphasizes that when there is no imam, the community must act out of necessity. Reflect on a time when you had to take responsibility because no one else would. How does that connect to the principle of tahkim discussed here?

Refuting the Fitna Objection Against Election
Refutes the claim that election causes civil strife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni refutation dismantles the argument that election causes civil strife, using the 'Ali and Mu'awiya example.

Core Idea
Election can resolve fitna; it is rebellion that causes strife, not the method of selection.

The third Shia objection argues that the imamate is meant to remove fitna, yet election itself stirs conflict, citing the dispute between 'Ali and Mu'awiya. This lesson presents the Sunni counter-argument that distinguishes between submission to truth, legitimate disagreement over procedure, and outright rebellion, showing that election is not the real cause of fitna.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if the imamate is to prevent fitna, then election—which caused fitna in the past—defeats the purpose.
The objection relies on a historical example to make its point.
2
First refutation: submission to truth
Notice the author's first response: fitna only occurs when people do not submit to the truth. The criteria for preference are known from Shari'ah, so disagreement is not inevitable.
3
Reinterpret the historical example
The author reinterprets the conflict between 'Ali and Mu'awiya: Mu'awiya did not dispute 'Ali's imamate itself, but whether allegiance was required before avenging 'Uthman's death. This was a political dispute, not a rejection of election.
This is a key distinction.
4
Final point: fitna from absence is worse
The author concludes that even if one accepts the objection, the discussion only applies when there is no nass. Moreover, the fitna caused by having no imam is far greater than any dispute over his selection.
This shifts the burden back to the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن الإمامة لإزالة الفتن وإثباتها بالبيعة مظنة إثارة الفتن لاختلاف الآراء، كما في زمن علي رضي الله تعالى عنه ومعاوية، فتعود على موضوعها بالنقض . ورد بأنه لا فتنة عند الانقياد للحق . فإن جهات الترجيح من السبق وغير معلومة من الشريعة . ونزاع معاوية لم يكن في إمامة علي رضي الله عنه بل في أنه هل يجب عليه بيعته قبل الاقتصاص من قتلة عثمان؟ وأما عند الترفع والاستيلاء فالفتنة قائمة ولو مع قيام النص . ولو سلم ، فالكلام فيما إذا لم يوجد النص إذ لا عبرة بالبيعة والاختيار على خلاف ما ورد به النص ولا خفاء في أن الفتنة القائمة من عدم الإمام أضعاف فتنة النزاع في تعيينه
Third: that the imamate is for removing civil strife (fitan), and establishing it by allegiance (bay'ah) is a likely cause of stirring up fitna due to difference of opinions, as in the time of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and Mu'awiya, so it defeats its own purpose. It is refuted by saying that there is no fitna when submitting to the truth. Indeed, the aspects of preponderance (tarijih) like precedence (sabq) and others are known from the Shari'ah. And the dispute of Mu'awiya was not about the imamate of Ali, but about whether allegiance was incumbent upon him before seeking retribution (qisas) from the murderers of Uthman. As for when there is arrogance and seizure, fitna is present even with the existence of nass. And if granted, the discussion is when there is no nass, for there is no regard for allegiance and choice contrary to what nass has stated, and it is no secret that the fitna arising from the absence of an imam is many times greater than the fitna of dispute over his appointment.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shia objection and its refutation. The objection argues that the imamate is meant to remove civil strife (fitan), but election (bay'ah) itself is likely to cause fitna due to differing opinions—citing the conflict between Ali and Mu'awiya. This would defeat the purpose of the imamate. The refutation makes several points: (1) Fitna does not arise when people submit to the truth; the criteria for preferring one candidate (like precedence) are known from the Shari'ah, so disagreement is not inevitable. (2) The conflict between Ali and Mu'awiya was not about the legitimacy of Ali's imamate but about whether Mu'awiya should give allegiance before avenging Uthman's death—a political dispute. (3) When people revolt and seize power, fitna exists even if the imam is designated by nass (as with Ali and Mu'awiya), so election is not the cause. (4) Even if one accepts the objection, the discussion is limited to cases where there is no nass; if nass exists, it must be followed. (5) The fitna resulting from having no imam at all is far greater than any dispute over his selection. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الفتنة
البيعة
النص
جهات الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conflict between 'Ali and Mu'awiya proves that election causes civil strife and therefore the imamate must be by nass.
Correction: The conflict was not about the legitimacy of 'Ali's imamate but about the timing of allegiance; even with nass, fitna can occur due to rebellion.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the conflict between 'Ali and Mu'awiya in response to the fitna objection?
A) Mu'awiya rejected 'Ali's imamate outright
B) The dispute was about whether Mu'awiya should give allegiance before avenging 'Uthman's death
C) 'Ali was not the rightful imam
D) The conflict proves election is invalid

Key Takeaway
Election does not cause fitna; it is rebellion and refusal to submit to truth that cause strife, and the fitna from having no imam is far worse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a disagreement about leadership or authority in your community, do you submit to the truth as established by the Shari'ah, or do you let your personal opinions cause division? What specific step will you take this week to ensure you are not a source of fitna?
?
The refutation argues that the fitna from having no imam is far greater than the fitna of disagreement over his appointment. Are there areas in your life where you avoid making a decision to avoid conflict, only to allow a greater chaos to emerge? What decision have you been postponing that you need to make today?
?
Muawiya's dispute was about a procedural matter, not about the core legitimacy of Ali's imamate. Do you sometimes confuse disagreements about procedures with disagreements about fundamental principles? How can you distinguish between the two in your relationships, and what will you do to mend a relationship where such confusion exists?

The Fourth Shia Objection: Imamate as Divine Caliphate
Refutes the claim that imamate must be directly from God and Prophet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia claim that imamate requires direct divine appointment and the Sunni refutation based on consensus and analogy.

Core Idea
Consensus makes the elected imam a divine caliph.

The fourth Shia objection argues that because the imam is a successor of God and the Prophet, only God or the Prophet can appoint him. Election by the community would make him a caliph of the community, not of God. The response shows that consensus, as a proof from the Lawgiver, transforms the community's choice into a divine appointment, drawing a parallel to how judicial rulings become God's rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly and then the English translation. Identify the main claim and the response.
Notice the compact argument style.
2
Identify objection and refutation
Locate the Shia objection (the part before 'wuridda') and the refutation that follows. Understand how consensus is used to counter the objection.
The refutation uses the term 'ijma' (consensus) as a proof from the Lawgiver.
3
Analyze the analogy
Study the analogy with the witness, judge, and mufti. How does it support the refutation? The rulings of these individuals are considered God's rulings despite being human actions.
Similarly, the imam's appointment by the community is considered a divine caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن الإمامة خلافة الله ورسوله فتتوقف على استخلافهما بوسط أو لا بوسط . والثابت باختيار الأمة لا يكون خلافة منهما ، بل من الأمة، ورد بأنه لما قام الدليل من قبل الشارع وهو الإجماع على أن من اختاره الأمة خليفة الله ورسوله، كان خليفة سقط ما ذكرتم ألا ترى أن الوجوب بشهادة الشاهد وقضاء القاضي، وفتوى المفتي حكم الله لا حكمهم على أن الإمام وإن كان نائباً لله فهو نائب للأمة ايضاً .
Fourth: that the imamate is the caliphate of God and His Messenger, so it depends on their appointment (istikhlaf) either through an intermediary or without. And what is established by the choice of the community cannot be a caliphate from them, but rather from the community. It is refuted by saying that when the proof from the Lawgiver is established—namely, the consensus that whoever the community chooses is the caliph of God and His Messenger—then he is a caliph, and what you mentioned falls away. Do you not see that the obligation by the testimony of a witness, the judgment of a judge, and the fatwa of a mufti is the judgment of God, not theirs, and also that the imam, though he is a deputy of God, is a deputy of the community as well?
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Shia objection and its refutation. The objection argues that the imamate is a succession from God and the Prophet, so it must be established by their appointment (istikhlaf) either directly or through an intermediary. Election by the community results in a caliphate from the community, not from God and the Prophet. The refutation counters that the Lawgiver (God) has provided proof—namely, consensus (ijma')—that the person chosen by the community is indeed the caliph of God and His Messenger. Therefore, the elected imam is a divine caliph, and the objection collapses. The author strengthens this by analogy: the obligation arising from a witness's testimony, a judge's verdict, and a mufti's fatwa is considered God's ruling, not the human's, even though humans are the agents. Similarly, the imam is a caliph from God and also from the community, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الخلافة
الإجماع
الشاهد
القاضي
المفتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation denies that the imam is a deputy of God.
Correction: The refutation affirms that the imam is a deputy of God via consensus, not that he is not a deputy.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that election cannot make the imam a divine caliph?
A) By arguing that divine appointment is unnecessary
B) By citing consensus that the elected imam is God's caliph, and analogizing to judicial rulings
C) By saying the Prophet explicitly designated the imam
D) By denying the concept of caliphate

Key Takeaway
Consensus (ijma') establishes that the community's choice is a divine appointment, making the imam a true caliph of God and His Messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader chosen by the Muslim community (such as a caliph or imam), do you truly accept that their authority is from God, or do you dismiss it as mere human politics? How does this lesson challenge you to recognize divine appointment in collective decisions?
?
Have you ever rejected a religious ruling or leadership because it came through human consensus rather than a direct text? What would change if you saw consensus as God's own proof, as stated in this passage?
?
In your personal life, do you seek divine validation only in miraculous or direct ways, or can you accept that God works through the community's agreement? Examine your heart: are you willing to submit to communal decisions even when they contradict your personal preference?
